عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
وبعضهم : يكثرون بإيراد الأسئلة والأجوبة. وبعضهم يطولون بإيراد الفروع الفقهية.
(1)
وقد كنت حين أقرأ «شرح الوقاية» على حضرة الوالد العلام أدخله الله دار السلام، كتبت عليه تعليقاً بأمره الشريف، حاوياً على حل بعض المقامات على حسب تقريره المنيف، ثمّ لَمَّا ترقى بي الحال، وترفعت من الحضيض إلى أوج الكمال، رأيته لا يغني لطالبه باختصاره، ولا يفيد للكملة باقتصاره ). فشرعت في شرح كبير مسمّى بـ «السعاية ) في كشف ما في شرح الوقاية»، التزمت فيه ترصيص المسائل بالدلائل وتأسيس المنقول بالمعقول، وضبط الفروع بالأصول، مع ذكر اختلاف الصحابة والتابعين ومن بعدهم من المجتهدين، وإيراد أدلتهم على مسلكهم، مع النقض والإبرام والجرح والإحكام على شريطة الإنصاف من دون التعصب والاعتساف، وأرجو من الله الكريم الذي وفقنا لهذا الأمر العظيم أن يفسح من عمري ويتم أمري، ويكمل شرحي ويتم قصدي، ويجعله ذريعة لنفع عباده ، وحكماً مصلحاً عند المنازعة بين عباده.
ثم طلب مني بعضُ خُلّص الأحباب وأجلَّةِ الأصحاب أن أحشي «شرح الوقاية»، وأُعلق عليه تعليقاً مختصراً من «السعاية، فبادرت إلى إجابة ملتمسهم ، وإنجاح مقترحهم ، ظناً مني أن كتاب «السعاية» لكونه مشتملاً على ما ذكرناه يطول الزمانُ في ، والتعجيل في نشر العلم بقدر الإمكان أولى من إبطائه، فكتبت عليه تعليقاً
اختتامه
سميته : سميته :
الثامنة
من
المئة الثالثة
«عمدة الرعاية في حل شرح الوقاية»
(2) سمعت أن بعض أبناء الزمان اعترضوا على تسميتي شرحي بـ«السعاية»، وقال: إن «السعاية» في اللغة بمعنى النميمة، وهذا عجيب منه دال على جهله باللغة وبكتب الشريعة، فإن كتب الفقه والحديث متطابقة على إيراد هذا اللفظ بمعنى السعي كما لا يخفى على من طالع أبواب العتق والمكاتب والوصايا وغير ذلك، وفي الدلائل في أوصاف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم المخصوص بشرف السعاية وفي كتب اللغة يقال : سعى سعيا وسعاية منه رحمه الله تعالى.

مقدمة الإمام اللكنوي
التزمت فيها :
.. حل المتن والشرح مع ذكر الجرح والدفع.
٢. وذكر أدلة الأحكام الفقهيّة من الكتاب أو السنة النبوية أو آثار الصحابة والأصول
المرضية.
. مع ذكر اختلاف الأئمة الحنفية، من دون اهتمام بذكر اختلافات غيرهـم مـن الأئمة المرضية.
٤. بالغت فيه في توضيح مطالب الشرح والمتن، وما يتعلق بهما من السؤال والجواب مع الضبط المستحسن.
ه. وأوردت حسب مناسبة المقام بعض الفروع التي يحتاج إليها غالباً.
٦. وأشرت إلى دفع الشبهات الواردة على مسائل الحنفية رمزاً وصراحة. وليس غرضي من هذا التأليف وسائر تأليفاتي - وكفى بالله شهيدا - الرياء والسمعة والافتخار، وإظهار الفضيلة فأي فخر لِمَن لا يدري ما يمضي عليه في القبر والحشر، وأي فضلِ لِمَن خُلِقَ من القَذَر، بل أن تنتفع به الطلبة، وتبتصر به ر به الكَمَلة، ويكون زادا نافعاً لي في سفر الآخرة ، وباعثاً لنجاتي من الأهوال الهائلة، وكثيراً ما أنشد(۱) قول
التاج السبكي
وتمايل
(۲)
وصرير أقلام
العلـــوم الــذلــي مـــن وصــل غـانـيـة وطـيـب عــــناق نل عويصة في الذهن أبلغ من مدامـــة ســــاقي على صفحاتها أشهى من الدوكاه والعشاق
Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah
And some of them: they multiply by bringing questions and answers. And some of them they lengthen by bringing fiqh branches.
(1)
And when I was reading 'Sharh al-Wiqayah' with my honored father, the scholar (may Allah enter him into the abode of peace), I wrote a commentary on it by his noble command, containing the solution of some passages according to his lofty exposition. Then when my situation advanced and I rose from the depths to the peak of perfection, I saw that it (the commentary) does not suffice its seeker due to its brevity, nor does it benefit the complete ones due to its conciseness. So I began a large commentary named 'al-Sa'ayah fi Kashf ma fi Sharh al-Wiqayah', in which I committed to fixing the issues with proofs, establishing transmitted knowledge with rational understanding, and controlling the branches with the principles, along with mentioning the differences of the Companions, the Followers, and the mujtahids after them, and adducing their evidences according to their methodology, with criticism and confirmation, rejection and validation, on the condition of fairness, without partisanship or injustice. And I ask Allah, the Generous, who has granted us success for this great matter, to extend my life, complete my affair, perfect my commentary, achieve my goal, and make it a means for the benefit of His servants, and a just ruling in disputes between His servants.
Then some of my close friends and noble companions asked me to write a gloss (hashiyah) on 'Sharh al-Wiqayah' and attach a brief commentary from 'al-Sa'ayah'. So I hastened to answer their request and fulfill their proposal, thinking that the book 'al-Sa'ayah'—since it contains what we mentioned—would take too long, and hastening to spread knowledge as much as possible is better than delaying it. So I wrote a commentary on it, and named it: 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah fi Hall Sharh al-Wiqayah'.
(2) I heard that some contemporaries objected to my naming my commentary 'al-Sa'ayah', saying: 'al-Sa'ayah in the language means slander (namimah).' This is strange from him, indicating his ignorance of the language and the books of the Shari'ah. For the books of fiqh and hadith are consistent in using this word in the sense of 'sai' (effort/endeavor), as is clear to anyone who studies the chapters of emancipation, the kitabah contract, bequests, and others. And in al-Dala'il, in the description of the Prophet (may Allah bless him and grant him peace), it is specifically distinguished by the honor of al-Sa'ayah. And in the language books, it is said: sa'a sa'yan wa sa'ayah - from him, may Allah have mercy on him.

Introduction of Imam al-Lakhnawi:
I committed in it:
1. Solving the matn and the sharh, along with mentioning criticism (jarh) and defense (daf').
2. Mentioning the evidences of fiqh rulings from the Book, the Prophetic Sunnah, the effects of the Companions, and the sound principles (al-usul al-mardiyyah).
3. Along with mentioning the differences of the Hanafi imams, without caring to mention the differences of other sound imams.
4. I went to great lengths in clarifying the demands of the sharh and the matn, and what relates to them of questions and answers, with good precision.
5. And I adduced, according to the appropriateness of the context, some branches that are often needed.
6. And I pointed out the refutation of doubts that arise about Hanafi issues, symbolically and explicitly.
And my purpose in this authorship and all my other writings—and Allah suffices as a witness—is not ostentation, fame, pride, or showing virtue. For what pride is there for one who does not know what will come upon him in the grave and the gathering? And what virtue for one created from filth? Rather, it is that the students benefit by it, the accomplished ones gain insight, and it becomes beneficial provision for me in the journey of the Hereafter, and a cause for my salvation from the terrible terrors. And often I recite the saying of Taj al-Subki:
And the screeching of pens on its pages is sweeter than the dolce [؟] and lovers
And the swaying of the lowly sciences from the union of a playful woman and fragrant embrace
He obtains a difficult matter in the mind that is more effective than the wine of a cupbearer
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This is the introduction of Imam Abd al-Hayy al-Lakhnawi to his work 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The passage type is front matter—the author sets forth his scholarly biography, motivation, and methodology. He begins by recounting his initial study of 'Sharh al-Wiqayah' under his father. He wrote a brief gloss at his father's command, but later felt it insufficient. So he composed a larger commentary, 'al-Sa'ayah fi Kashf ma fi Sharh al-Wiqayah', in which he committed to supporting each issue with proofs (dilail), integrating transmitted knowledge (manqul) with rational argument (ma'qul), and connecting furu' (branches) to usul (principles). He also promised to mention the differences among the Companions, Followers, and later mujtahids, along with their arguments per their own methodologies, engaging in criticism (jarh) and confirmation (ta'dil) with fairness and without partisanship. Then, at the request of friends, he extracted from 'al-Sa'ayah' a shorter gloss, which he named 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah fi Hall Sharh al-Wiqayah'. He also defends the name 'al-Sa'ayah' against critics who misunderstood it as 'slander'—he clarifies that in hadith and fiqh terminology it means effort/endeavor. Following this, he lists six commitments of this work: (1) solving the matn and sharh with critique and defense; (2) citing evidences from Quran, Sunnah, Companions' athar, and sound usul; (3) focusing on intra-Hanafi differences, ignoring other schools; (4) thorough clarification of questions and answers; (5) including frequently needed subsidiary issues; (6) refuting doubts about Hanafi positions implicitly and explicitly. He concludes by affirming his sincere intention: neither fame nor pride, but benefit for students, insight for scholars, and provision for the Hereafter. He quotes a couplet by Taj al-Subki expressing the sweetness of scholarly labor over worldly pleasures. This passage shows the author's rigorous scholarly self-awareness and his commitment to the Hanafi madhhab within the tradition of commentary and super-commentary.

Key Terms
شرح الوقاية (Sharh al-Wiqayah): The commentary on 'Al-Wiqayah' by Ubayd Allah al-Mahbubi, a foundational Hanafi text. This is the base text upon which al-Lakhnawi writes his gloss.
الجرح والدفع (al-Jarh wa al-Daf'): Critique and refutation. The author commits to both criticizing weaker positions (jarh) and defending stronger ones (daf') in his commentary.
الأصول المرضية (al-Usul al-Mardiyyah): The sound/approved principles of jurisprudence, specifically those accepted by the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the introduction, which of the following is NOT one of the six commitments the author states in this work?
A) Solving the matn and sharh with criticism and defense
B) Mentioning evidences from Quran, Sunnah, Companions' athar, and sound usul
C) Mentioning differences among all four madhhabs
D) Refuting doubts about Hanafi positions

Key Takeaway
Al-Lakhnawi's introduction establishes a rigorous, proof-based Hanafi commentary method focused on internal school differences, critical fairness, and sincere intention for Allah's pleasure.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
The Six Commitments of Imam al-Laknawi's Methodology
Author outlines six commitments of his commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six commitments of Imam al-Laknawi's methodology in 'Umdat al-Ri'aya and explain how each shapes the commentary.

Core Idea
Imam al-Laknawi's commentary is built on six commitments: resolving texts, citing evidence, focusing on Hanafi differences, clarifying demands, including subsidiary rulings, and refuting doubts.

Every scholarly work has a purpose and method. In his introduction to 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, Imam al-Laknawi lays out six commitments that guide his supercommentary. Understanding these commitments will help you navigate the text and appreciate the author's approach to Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six commitments
Read the Arabic passage and list the six numbered commitments. Each begins with a verb (التزمت) or an implied action.
The first commitment is implied after the colon; look for key phrases like 'حل المتن والشرح' and 'ذكر أدلة الأحكام'.
2
Examine each commitment's purpose
For each commitment, ask: Why does the author prioritize this? What problem does it solve in fiqh scholarship?
For example, 'الجرح والدفع' addresses criticisms of Hanafi positions.
3
Understand the author's sincere motivation
Read the author's disclaimer about avoiding ostentation and his goal of benefiting students and seeking salvation.
The quoted poems illustrate his dedication and disdain for worldly fame.
4
Recognize the target audience
The author addresses 'الطلبة' (students) and 'الكملة' (the perfect ones). Who are these?
Students of knowledge and advanced scholars seeking insight.
5
Connect to the Hanafi tradition
Commitment 3 emphasizes focusing solely on Hanafi internal differences. How does this shape the commentary's scope?
This makes the work a specialized Hanafi reference, not a comparative fiqh text.

مقدمة الإمام اللكنوي
التزمت فيها :
.. حل المتن والشرح مع ذكر الجرح والدفع.
٢. وذكر أدلة الأحكام الفقهيّة من الكتاب أو السنة النبوية أو آثار الصحابة والأصول المرضية.
٣. مع ذكر اختلاف الأئمة الحنفية، من دون اهتمام بذكر اختلافات غيرهم من الأئمة المرضية.
٤. بالغت فيه في توضيح مطالب الشرح والمتن، وما يتعلق بهما من السؤال والجواب مع الضبط المستحسن.
ه. وأوردت حسب مناسبة المقام بعض الفروع التي يحتاج إليها غالباً.

صرير أقلام
العلوم الذي من وصل غانية وطيب عناق
نل عويصة في الذهن أبلغ من مدامة ساقي
على صفحاتها أشهى من الدوكاه والعشاق
وقول محمد الدمشقي المحاسني أستاذ العلاء الحصكفي:
لكل بنى الدنيا مراد ومقصد وإن مرادي صحة وفراغ
لأبلغ في علم الشريعة مبلغاً يكون لي به في الحات بلاغ

وكان ذلك حين كنت مرهوناً بالإحسانات الفائضة إلي، وممنوناً بالإنعامات الواصلة لديّ من حضرةِ مَن هو بدر بدور الوزارة شمس شموس الرئاسة، باسط بساط العدل والإنصاف، قامع بنيان الظلم والاعتساف، الذي سطعت أنوار العلم والهداية بلطفه ؛ ورعدت سحائب الفضل والدراية بفضله، عتبته الرفيعة ملجأ للعلماء، وسدته العليّة مأوى للفضلاء.

Introduction of Imam al-Laknawi. I have committed in it: 1. Resolving the matn and the sharh while mentioning criticism and defense. 2. Mentioning the evidences of fiqh rulings from the Book, the Prophetic Sunnah, the traditions of the Companions, and the sound principles. 3. Along with mentioning the differences of the Hanafi imams, without concern for mentioning the differences of others among the sound imams. 4. I have exerted effort in it to clarify the demands of the sharh and the matn, and what relates to them of questions and answers with good recording. 5. And I have presented, according to the suitability of the context, some subsidiary issues that are often needed. 6. And I have pointed to repelling doubts that arise on Hanafi issues, symbolically and explicitly. And my purpose in this authorship and all my other writings - and sufficient is Allah as a witness - is not ostentation, fame, pride, or showing off virtue; for what pride for one who does not know what will pass over him in the grave and the Resurrection, and what virtue for one who was created from filth. Rather, it is that students may benefit from it, the perfect ones may gain insight through it, and it may be a beneficial provision for me in the journey of the Hereafter, and a motivator for my salvation from the terrible terrors. I often recite the saying of Taj al-Subki: 'The creaking of pens on pages is sweeter than the wine of a cup-bearer. The sloping of the sciences, which is from the union of a beloved and a fragrant embrace, get a problem in the mind more effective than the wine of a server.' And the saying of Muhammad al-Dimashqi al-Mahasini, the teacher of al-‘Ala’ al-Haskafi: 'For every builder of this world is a goal and purpose, but my goal is health and leisure to attain in the science of Sharia a level through which I will have in the gatherings a communication. So in such things let the people of understanding compete. And sufficient for me from this world is the deception. Success is only in eternal bliss with a comfortable life and drink that is palatable.' And that was when I was indebted to the abundant favors that overflowed to me, and obligated by the bounties that reached me from his presence who is the full moon of the ministry, the sun of the leadership, who spreads the carpet of justice and fairness, who demolishes the building of oppression and transgression, by whose kindness the lights of knowledge and guidance have shone; and by whose virtue the clouds of excellence and sagacity have thundered. His lofty threshold is a refuge for scholars, and his exalted seat is a shelter for the virtuous. By his generosity, the palaces of Sharia rose at the time of their decline, and the people of knowledge ascended the ascents of Sharia in the time of its weakness. He is the possessor of high positions and noble virtues, the Asaf of the sultanate.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction (front matter) to his supercommentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on Sharh al-Wiqaya. Imam al-Laknawi enumerates six key commitments of his work: first, to resolve differences between the matn (original text) and the sharh (commentary) while addressing both criticism and defense of positions. Second, to cite evidence from Quran, Sunnah, Companions' traditions, and sound principles of fiqh. Third, to focus exclusively on internal Hanafi differences (ikhtilafat among the Hanafi imams), ignoring disagreements with other schools. Fourth, to thoroughly explain the demands of both the matn and sharh, incorporating questions and answers with careful precision. Fifth, to include frequently needed subsidiary rulings (furu') appropriate to the context. Sixth, to implicitly and explicitly refute doubts raised against Hanafi rulings. He then declares that his motive is not worldly gain but sincere benefit for students and personal salvation, quoting two poems: one by Taj al-Subki praising the pleasure of scholarly writing over worldly delights, and another by al-Muhasini expressing his dedication to mastering Sharia for eternal bliss. Finally, he praises his patron, the minister of his time, extolling his support for Islamic knowledge and justice.

Key Terms
الجرح والدفع (al-jarh wa al-daf‘): Criticism and defense; the author aims to address objections to rulings and defend the Hanafi position.
أدلة الأحكام الفقهيّة (adillat al-ahkam al-fiqhiyyah): Evidences for fiqh rulings, including Quran, Sunnah, and principles.
اختلاف الأئمة الحنفية (ikhtilaf al-a'immah al-hanafiyyah): Differences among Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, etc.).
الفروع (al-furu‘): Subsidiary rulings derived from principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many main commitments does Imam al-Laknawi state in his introduction for this work?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Laknawi's six-commitment methodology ensures that his commentary is both evidence-based and focused on defending Hanafi positions.

Understanding the Author's Introduction and Dedication in Hanafi Scholarly Works
Author's introductory praise of ruler and patron

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a classical Hanafi scholar's introduction and explain the purpose of patron dedication.

Core Idea
A scholar's introduction is not fiqh – it is a historical and biographical preface that situates the work within its patronage and scholarly tradition.

This passage is the opening of Imam 'Abd al-Hayy al-Laknawi's commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. Before diving into the fiqh of al-Wiqaya, al-Laknawi begins with a lengthy eulogy of his patron, the Nizamiyya ruler Mahbub 'Ali Khan. This is typical of classical Hanafi works: the author acknowledges the support of a ruler, praises his lineage, and then asks for the reader's prayers. While this section contains no legal rulings, it is important for understanding the historical context in which Hanafi fiqh was produced and transmitted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and his patron
Who is the author? Who is the work dedicated to? Look for names and titles.
The patron is Mahbub 'Ali Khan, with titles like Asaf Jah Bahadur.
2
Analyze the purpose of the eulogy
Why does the author spend so much time praising the ruler? This reveals the relationship of patronage that supported the scholarly work.
The ruler is called 'a refuge for groups of learned men and a shelter for them'.
3
Note the scholarly petitions
What does the author ask of his readers? This shows scholarly humility and the communal nature of knowledge.
He asks for thanks, prayers, and forgiveness for errors.
4
Recognize the structure of the introduction
How does the author transition from praise to the actual work? He announces an introduction with many benefits.
The phrase 'هذا أوان الشروع في المقصود' marks the transition.

مقدمة الإمام اللكنوي النظامية، وزير الدولة الآصفيّة : النواب مختار الملك سالارجنك تراب عليخان بهادر، لا زالت بدور إقباله بازغة وشموس أفضاله طالعة، وذلك في عهد سلطنة سلطان الإسلام، ظلّ الله على الأنام، رافع ألوية الولاية في الآفاق، مالك أسرة الخلافة بالاستحقاق، نور حديقة الرئاسة العظمى ، نور حديقة الإمارة الكبرى، بدر بروج الجلالة، قمر منازل السيادة، الذي يحوم حول منزله العالمون، ويزدحم حول عتبته العالمون، حقيق بأن ينشد في حقه ما أنشده السعد التفتازاني في شأن ملكه : علا فأصبح يدعوه الورى ملكا وريثما فتحوا عيناً غدا ملكا هو السلطان بن السلطان بن السلطان، والخاقان من الخاقان من الخاقان سلطان السلطنة النظاميّة، مالك الرئاسة الآصفية : النواب محبوب عليخان محبوب الدولة ظفر الممالك فتح جنك نظام الملك آصف جاه بهادر، أدام الله سطوته وشوكته، وأعلى الله درجته ورتبته ، ابن النواب أفضل الدولة ابن النواب ناصر الدولة ، نور الله مرقدهما ، وبرَّدَ مضجعهما، وبعدما أتممت هذا التعليق الأنيق ، خدمته بحضرته الشريفة، وأتحفته بعتبته الرفيعة، لا زالت ملجأ لطوائف الأعلام ، وملاذاً لهم من حوادث الليالي والأيام والمرجو ممن يطالعه، وينتفع به أن يشكروني فيما تحملته في ترصيفه من المحنة في ظما الهواجر والمشقة في ظلم الدياجر، وأن يدعو لي بحسن الأوائل والأواخر وبالنجاة عن أهوال المحشر والمقابر، وأن يصفحوا عن خطأ الأقلام، وزلة الأقدام إن وقفوا عليه، فإنّ الإنسان ملازم للسهو والنسيان، وقد جبل عليه، والله تعالى أسألُ سؤال الضارع الخاشع ، متوسلاً بحبيبه المشفّع الشافع أن يتقبل هذا التصنيف وسائر تأليفاتي، ويجعلها ذخيرة لمعادي، إنّه بالإجابة جدير، وعلى كل شيء قدير، هذا أوان الشروع في المقصود، متوكلاً على الرب الودود. ولنقدّم مقدمة تشمل على فوائد مهمة تنفعُ للطلاب، وتشرح صدور أولي الألباب، مرتبة على دراسات عديدة فيها لطائف سديدة، المقدمة متضمنة على دراسات متعددة :
Introduction of Imam al-Laknawi: al-Nizamiyya, the minister of the Asafiyya state: the noble Mukhtar al-Malik Salarjung Turab 'Ali Khan Bahadur, may his good fortune never cease to shine and the suns of his favors rise, and that in the reign of the Sultan of Islam, the shadow of Allah over mankind, the raiser of the banners of wilaya in the horizons, the rightful owner of the caliphal lineage, the light of the garden of the great presidency, the light of the garden of the great emirate, the full moon of the constellations of majesty, the moon of the stages of sovereignty, around whose dwelling the worlds revolve, around whose threshold the worlds crowd, deserving that what al-Sa'd al-Taftazani composed about his king be recited in his honor: 'He rose so high that people call him king, and when they opened an eye, he became king.' He is the sultan, son of the sultan, son of the sultan, and the khaqan, son of the khaqan, son of the khaqan: the sultan of the Nizamiyya sultanate, the owner of the Asafiyya presidency: the noble Mahbub 'Ali Khan Mahbub al-Dawla Zafar al-Mamalik Fath Jung Nizam al-Mulk Asaf Jah Bahadur, may Allah perpetuate his might and power, and elevate his rank and station, son of the noble Afdal al-Dawla, son of the noble Nasir al-Dawla, may Allah illuminate their graves and cool their resting places. After I completed this elegant commentary, I presented it to his noble presence and offered it at his lofty threshold, which remains a refuge for groups of learned men and a shelter for them from the vicissitudes of nights and days. I hope that whoever reads it and benefits from it will thank me for the hardship I endured in composing it: the toil of noon-time heat and the difficulty of dark nights, and that he will pray for me for good outcomes in the beginning and the end, for deliverance from the terrors of the gathering and the graves, and that he will overlook errors of pens and slips of feet if he comes across them, for man is ever prone to inadvertence and forgetfulness, and is created thus. I ask Allah Almighty, with the supplication of the humble and submissive, imploring through His beloved intercessor and pleader, to accept this work and all my other compositions, and to make them a provision for my Hereafter. He is worthy of answer, and over all things powerful. This is the time to begin the main subject, relying on the Loving Lord. We will present an introduction containing important benefits that will help students and expand the hearts of those of understanding, arranged in numerous studies containing sound subtleties. The introduction comprises multiple studies:
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is the author's front matter, where he begins with an elaborate eulogy and dedication. He first praises the ruler and patron under whose reign and patronage the work was completed, listing his titles and lineage. He then makes a scholarly petition, asking readers for their appreciation and prayers, and acknowledges human fallibility. Finally, he announces the beginning of the actual work and states that he will preface it with an introduction containing beneficial points for students. This is typical of classical Islamic scholarly works, where authors often begin with praise of Allah, blessings upon the Prophet, and then a dedication to a patron or ruler. The introduction is not a legal ruling but a scholarly preface.

Key Terms
النواب (al-nawwāb): Title meaning 'deputy' or 'lord', used as an honorific for high-ranking officials in the Mughal/Asafiyya context.
السلطنة النظامية (al-saltanah al-nizāmiyyah): The Nizamiyya Sultanate, a historical polity in South India under the Asaf Jahi dynasty.
التعليق الأنيق (al-ta'līq al-anīq): This elegant commentary; refers to the author's own work (the supercommentary on al-Wiqaya).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the author's introduction, what does he ask from his readers?
A) To accept the work without criticism
B) To thank him and pray for him
C) To spread his name among rulers
D) To memorize the entire commentary

Key Takeaway
The author's introduction provides historical context and shows the relationship between scholars and patrons, but it does not contain legal rulings.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
The Transmission of Knowledge and the Justice of the Companions
Front matter establishing transmission and adala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Hanafi school considers the Companions to be just and explain how this ensures the reliability of transmitted knowledge.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school's authority flows from the unbroken chain of trustworthy narrators, beginning with the just Companions.

This opening passage establishes the foundation of the Hanafi methodology: the transmission of knowledge from the Prophet through the Companions, who are universally accepted as just. The author quotes al-Kafawi and al-Amidi to prove their integrity, which is essential for accepting the hadith and practices that form the basis of the school.

Guided Path
1
Trace the chain of transmission
Identify the sequence: Prophet → Companions → Followers → later scholars. Note how each generation preserved and passed on the religion.
2
Understand the proof of adalat al-sahaba
Examine the Quranic verses and scholarly consensus cited by the author to establish the justice of all Companions. This is a Sunni principle.
3
Connect to the Hanafi school's authority
Realize that the entire edifice of Hanafi fiqh relies on the trustworthy transmission from the Companions. If any Companion were suspect, the chain would be broken.

الصواب كما شهدت الأدلة الواضحة الصريحة وكلمات العلماء والأئمة الكبار، فلا يغتر بخلاف ذلك، وقد بسطت هذا في سبيل الوصول إلى علم الأصول» (ص۱۱۲) من ذلك قول الآمدي في «الإحكام» (۲ : ۱۰۲ ۱۰۳): «والمختار إنما هو مذهب الجمهور من الأئمة وذلك بما تحقق من الأدلة الدالة على عدالتهم ونزاهتهم وتخييرهم على من بعدهم، فمن ذلك قوله : وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطًا [البقرة : ١٤٣]: أي عدولاً ، وقوله : كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ (آل عمران : ١١٠]، وهو خطاب مع ا الصحابة الموجودين في زمن النبي ، ولأن ما ظهر واشتهر بالنقل المتواتر الذي لا مراء فيه من مناصرتهم للرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم والهجرة إليه والجهاد بين يديه والمحافظة على أمور الدين، وإقامة القوانين، والتشدد في امتثال أوامر الشرع ونواهيه، والقيام بحدوده ومراسيمه، حتى أنهم قتلوا الأهل والأولاد حتى قام الدين واستقام ولا أدل على العدالة أكثر من ذلك ، وعند ذلك فالواجب أن يحمل كل ما جرى بينهم من الفتن على أحسن حال، وإن كان ذلك إنّما لما أدى إليه اجتهاد كل فريق من ال أن الواجب ما صار إليه ، وأنه أوفق للدين وأصلح للمسلمين».
اعتقاده

وكذا أعلام التابعين الذين هم يزاحمونهم في الفتوى ، ووافقوا لهم بغير خلاف، ونقلوا أحكام الدين منهم إلى الأخلاف، محيين سَنَن الأسلاف، حاوين مآثر الأشراف. ولما كانت حوادث الأيام خارجة عن التعداد، ومعرفة أحكامها لازمة إلى يوم
التناد، وكانت ظواهر النصوص غير موفية ببيانها ، بل لا بد لها من طريق واف بشأنها اضطروا إلى الاجتهاد بالرأي ، فاجتهدوا وأسَّسُوا قواعد الأصول وشيدوا، فعزموا على تعيين المذهب، ومهدوا مستفيضين بما روي عن رسول الله ل له أَنه لَمَّا بعث معاذه إلى اليمن قاضياً، قال له : «بم تقضي يا معاذ؟ قال : بكتاب الله ، قال : فإن لم تجد؟ قال : بسنة رسوله، قال : فإن لم تجد ؟ قال : اجتهد فيه برأيي، فقال رسول الله : الحمد الله الذي وفق رسول رسوله بما يرضى به رسوله (۱).
ثم إن علماء الدين والأئمة المجتهدين بذلوا جهدهم في تحقيق المسائل الشرعية، وتدقيق النظائر الفرعيّة، واستنبطوا أحكام الفروع عن الأدلة الأربعة، فاتفاقهم حجة قاطعة، واختلافهم رحمة واسعة، فمنهم أصحاب الطبقة العالية من الاجتهاد، وهم الذين صادف الدين بهم أقوى عماد ، وضعوا المسائل على قواعد أصولهم، وهذبوا مسائل الاجتهاد، مع تنقيح طرق النظر على مذاهبهم ، يستمدون في استنباط الأحكام من الكتاب والسنة والإجماع والقياس من غير تقليد في الأصول ولا في الفروع لأحدٍ من الناس، وحالهم متفاوتة في اشتهار مذاهبهم، واعتبار مشاربهم.
(1) أخرجه أبو داود (۳) : (۳۱۳) والترمذي (۳): (٦١٦ وأشار إلى ضعفه وله شواهد موقوفة عن عمر وابن مسعود وزيد بن ثابت وابن عباس الله أخرجهما البيهقي في سننه»(١٠ : ١١٤) عقیب تخريج هذا الحديث تقوية له. كذا في مرقاة الصعود شرح سنن أبي داود» للسيوطي. منه رحمه
الله تعالى.
أقول : قال الخطيب في «الفقيه والمتفقه» (۱ : ۱۸۸) : «إن أهل العلم قد تقبلوه واحتجوا به فوقفنا بذلك على صحته عندهم كما وقفنا على صحة قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «لا وصية لوارث، وقوله في البحر : هو الطهور ماؤه الحل ميتته»، وقوله : «إذا اختلف المتبايعان في الثمن والسلعة قائمة تحالفا وترادا، وقوله : «الدية على العاقل ، وإن كانت هذه الأحاديث لا تثبت من جهة الإسناد لكن لما تلقتها الكافة عن الكافة غنوا بصحتها عندهم عن طلب الإسناد لها فكذلك حديث معاذ لما احتجوا به جميعاً غنوا عن طلب الإسناد له. وتمامه في هامش الحدود والأحكام الفقهية (ص۸۲
.(۸۳
The First Study: On how knowledge spread from the presence of the Message to our time, and the spread of the schools of the mujtahids, especially the school of Imam Abu Hanifa. Al-Kafawi said in his Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya: 'Know that our Prophet conveyed what was revealed to us, taught the religion, established the hudud, judged and ruled, explained the Sharia, detailed the explanation of rulings, and strove in establishing the matter of religion. Then the Rightly Guided Caliphs and eminent Companions exerted their efforts in establishing religion, executing the clear Sharia, setting the foundations of the monotheists, and weakening the plots of God's enemies, the innovators. They established Islam upright and entrusted the affair to its foundation, holding fast to God's help, and declaring God's command. They were, by the honor of companionship, safe from criticism, and by the blessing of service, free from blemish. So their effects for those after them were a clear path and a methodology, and to remove the darkness of misguidance.' Then he discusses the justice of the Companions, quoting al-Amidi from al-Ihkam, citing verses of the Quran and the consensus of the community. Then: 'Likewise the notable Followers who competed with them in fatwa, agreed with them without dispute, and transmitted the rulings of religion from them to the successors, reviving the ways of the predecessors, encompassing the merits of the nobles. Since events of the days are beyond count, and knowledge of their rulings is necessary until the Day of Summoning, and the apparent meanings of texts are insufficient to explain them, they were compelled to ijtihad by opinion. So they performed ijtihad and established the foundations of usul and strengthened them. They resolved to determine the madhhab, relying on what was narrated from the Messenger of God that when he sent Mu'adh to Yemen as a judge, he said: 'By what will you judge, O Mu'adh?' He said: 'By the Book of God.' He said: 'If you do not find?' He said: 'By the Sunnah of His Messenger.' He said: 'If you do not find?' He said: 'I will exercise ijtihad with my opinion.' So the Messenger of God said: 'Praise be to God who has guided the messenger of His Messenger to what pleases His Messenger.' Then the scholars and mujtahid imams exerted their efforts in verifying legal issues, scrutinizing subsidiary parallels, and deriving subsidiary rulings from the four proofs. Their agreement is a decisive proof, and their disagreement is a vast mercy. Among them are the highest class of ijtihad, who placed issues on the foundations of their usul, refined the issues of ijtihad, and clarified methods of reasoning according to their schools, deriving rulings from the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy, without taqlid in usul or furu' to anyone. Their states vary in the fame of their schools and consideration of their ways.'
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is the opening section of the book, serving as a front matter establishing the authority and methodology of the Hanafi school. It begins by quoting al-Kafawi on the transmission of knowledge from the Prophet through the Companions and followers. The text emphasizes the justice (adala) of the Companions, which is a fundamental Sunni belief, and cites verses from the Quran and the consensus of scholars to support it. This is important because the reliability of the transmission chain depends on the integrity of the Companions. The passage then explains the necessity of ijtihad: since new events arise constantly, and the texts (Quran and Sunnah) do not cover every detail explicitly, qualified scholars must exert their own reasoning (ijtihad bi al-ra'y). The hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal is presented as a proof for the legitimacy of ijtihad. The text also addresses the authenticity of this hadith, noting that although its chain is weak, scholars have accepted it due to its widespread acceptance and use as evidence. Finally, it describes the highest class of mujtahids, who derive rulings directly from the primary sources without taqlid. This sets the stage for the Hanafi school's claim to be based on sound methodology and continuous transmission.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): The exertion of mental effort by a qualified scholar to derive legal rulings from the primary sources.
عدالة الصحابة (adalat al-sahaba): The moral integrity and trustworthiness of the Companions, making their testimony reliable.
المذهب (al-madhhab): The legal school, here specifically the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence the author uses to prove the justice of the Companions?
A) A single hadith about the Companions
B) Quranic verses and consensus
C) The opinion of al-Kafawi alone
D) The practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rests on the undisputed justice of the Companions, ensuring the reliability of knowledge transmission.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Ijtihad in the Hanafi School: Justification and Levels
Argument for ijtihad and description of mujtahids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the hadith of Mu'adh as evidence for ijtihad and describe the highest level of mujtahids in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school legitimizes ijtihad through the Prophet's approval of Mu'adh's reasoning, and recognizes a hierarchy of scholars who derive rulings directly from primary sources.

This section of the introduction addresses the necessity of ijtihad: since daily events are infinite and texts finite, qualified scholars must use reasoning. The hadith of Mu'adh provides prophetic sanction for this method. The passage then describes the highest class of mujtahids, who are the architects of the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Understand why ijtihad is necessary
The text argues that new events are countless, and the apparent meanings of texts are insufficient to cover them all. Hence, ijtihad is required.
2
Analyze the hadith of Mu'adh
Study the dialogue between the Prophet and Mu'adh: judging by Quran, then Sunnah, then ijtihad. Note the Prophet's approval.
3
Recognize the school's approach to hadith authenticity
The author acknowledges the hadith's weak chain but argues for its acceptance due to widespread scholarly use and acceptance.
4
Identify the characteristics of the highest mujtahids
They derived rulings from the four proofs (Quran, Sunnah, consensus, analogy) without taqlid, and established the usul of their schools.

الصواب كما شهدت الأدلة الواضحة الصريحة وكلمات العلماء والأئمة الكبار، فلا يغتر بخلاف ذلك، وقد بسطت هذا في سبيل الوصول إلى علم الأصول» (ص۱۱۲) من ذلك قول الآمدي في «الإحكام» (۲ : ۱۰۲ ۱۰۳): «والمختار إنما هو مذهب الجمهور من الأئمة وذلك بما تحقق من الأدلة الدالة على عدالتهم ونزاهتهم وتخييرهم على من بعدهم، فمن ذلك قوله : وَكَذَلِكَ جَعَلْنَكُمْ أُمَّةً وَسَطًا [البقرة : ١٤٣]: أي عدولاً ، وقوله : كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ (آل عمران : ١١٠]، وهو خطاب مع ا الصحابة الموجودين في زمن النبي ، ولأن ما ظهر واشتهر بالنقل المتواتر الذي لا مراء فيه من مناصرتهم للرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم والهجرة إليه والجهاد بين يديه والمحافظة على أمور الدين، وإقامة القوانين، والتشدد في امتثال أوامر الشرع ونواهيه، والقيام بحدوده ومراسيمه، حتى أنهم قتلوا الأهل والأولاد حتى قام الدين واستقام ولا أدل على العدالة أكثر من ذلك ، وعند ذلك فالواجب أن يحمل كل ما جرى بينهم من الفتن على أحسن حال، وإن كان ذلك إنّما لما أدى إليه اجتهاد كل فريق من ال أن الواجب ما صار إليه ، وأنه أوفق للدين وأصلح للمسلمين».
اعتقاده

وكذا أعلام التابعين الذين هم يزاحمونهم في الفتوى ، ووافقوا لهم بغير خلاف، ونقلوا أحكام الدين منهم إلى الأخلاف، محيين سَنَن الأسلاف، حاوين مآثر الأشراف. ولما كانت حوادث الأيام خارجة عن التعداد، ومعرفة أحكامها لازمة إلى يوم
التناد، وكانت ظواهر النصوص غير موفية ببيانها ، بل لا بد لها من طريق واف بشأنها اضطروا إلى الاجتهاد بالرأي ، فاجتهدوا وأسَّسُوا قواعد الأصول وشيدوا، فعزموا على تعيين المذهب، ومهدوا مستفيضين بما روي عن رسول الله ل له أَنه لَمَّا بعث معاذه إلى اليمن قاضياً، قال له : «بم تقضي يا معاذ؟ قال : بكتاب الله ، قال : فإن لم تجد؟ قال : بسنة رسوله، قال : فإن لم تجد ؟ قال : اجتهد فيه برأيي، فقال رسول الله : الحمد الله الذي وفق رسول رسوله بما يرضى به رسوله (۱).
ثم إن علماء الدين والأئمة المجتهدين بذلوا جهدهم في تحقيق المسائل الشرعية، وتدقيق النظائر الفرعيّة، واستنبطوا أحكام الفروع عن الأدلة الأربعة، فاتفاقهم حجة قاطعة، واختلافهم رحمة واسعة، فمنهم أصحاب الطبقة العالية من الاجتهاد، وهم الذين صادف الدين بهم أقوى عماد ، وضعوا المسائل على قواعد أصولهم، وهذبوا مسائل الاجتهاد، مع تنقيح طرق النظر على مذاهبهم ، يستمدون في استنباط الأحكام من الكتاب والسنة والإجماع والقياس من غير تقليد في الأصول ولا في الفروع لأحدٍ من الناس، وحالهم متفاوتة في اشتهار مذاهبهم، واعتبار مشاربهم.
(1) أخرجه أبو داود (۳) : (۳۱۳) والترمذي (۳): (٦١٦ وأشار إلى ضعفه وله شواهد موقوفة عن عمر وابن مسعود وزيد بن ثابت وابن عباس الله أخرجهما البيهقي في سننه»(١٠ : ١١٤) عقیب تخريج هذا الحديث تقوية له. كذا في مرقاة الصعود شرح سنن أبي داود» للسيوطي. منه رحمه
الله تعالى.
أقول : قال الخطيب في «الفقيه والمتفقه» (۱ : ۱۸۸) : «إن أهل العلم قد تقبلوه واحتجوا به فوقفنا بذلك على صحته عندهم كما وقفنا على صحة قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «لا وصية لوارث، وقوله في البحر : هو الطهور ماؤه الحل ميتته»، وقوله : «إذا اختلف المتبايعان في الثمن والسلعة قائمة تحالفا وترادا، وقوله : «الدية على العاقل ، وإن كانت هذه الأحاديث لا تثبت من جهة الإسناد لكن لما تلقتها الكافة عن الكافة غنوا بصحتها عندهم عن طلب الإسناد لها فكذلك حديث معاذ لما احتجوا به جميعاً غنوا عن طلب الإسناد له. وتمامه في هامش الحدود والأحكام الفقهية (ص۸۲
.(۸۳
The First Study: On how knowledge spread from the presence of the Message to our time, and the spread of the schools of the mujtahids, especially the school of Imam Abu Hanifa. Al-Kafawi said in his Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya: 'Know that our Prophet conveyed what was revealed to us, taught the religion, established the hudud, judged and ruled, explained the Sharia, detailed the explanation of rulings, and strove in establishing the matter of religion. Then the Rightly Guided Caliphs and eminent Companions exerted their efforts in establishing religion, executing the clear Sharia, setting the foundations of the monotheists, and weakening the plots of God's enemies, the innovators. They established Islam upright and entrusted the affair to its foundation, holding fast to God's help, and declaring God's command. They were, by the honor of companionship, safe from criticism, and by the blessing of service, free from blemish. So their effects for those after them were a clear path and a methodology, and to remove the darkness of misguidance.' Then he discusses the justice of the Companions, quoting al-Amidi from al-Ihkam, citing verses of the Quran and the consensus of the community. Then: 'Likewise the notable Followers who competed with them in fatwa, agreed with them without dispute, and transmitted the rulings of religion from them to the successors, reviving the ways of the predecessors, encompassing the merits of the nobles. Since events of the days are beyond count, and knowledge of their rulings is necessary until the Day of Summoning, and the apparent meanings of texts are insufficient to explain them, they were compelled to ijtihad by opinion. So they performed ijtihad and established the foundations of usul and strengthened them. They resolved to determine the madhhab, relying on what was narrated from the Messenger of God that when he sent Mu'adh to Yemen as a judge, he said: 'By what will you judge, O Mu'adh?' He said: 'By the Book of God.' He said: 'If you do not find?' He said: 'By the Sunnah of His Messenger.' He said: 'If you do not find?' He said: 'I will exercise ijtihad with my opinion.' So the Messenger of God said: 'Praise be to God who has guided the messenger of His Messenger to what pleases His Messenger.' Then the scholars and mujtahid imams exerted their efforts in verifying legal issues, scrutinizing subsidiary parallels, and deriving subsidiary rulings from the four proofs. Their agreement is a decisive proof, and their disagreement is a vast mercy. Among them are the highest class of ijtihad, who placed issues on the foundations of their usul, refined the issues of ijtihad, and clarified methods of reasoning according to their schools, deriving rulings from the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy, without taqlid in usul or furu' to anyone. Their states vary in the fame of their schools and consideration of their ways.'
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is the opening section of the book, serving as a front matter establishing the authority and methodology of the Hanafi school. It begins by quoting al-Kafawi on the transmission of knowledge from the Prophet through the Companions and followers. The text emphasizes the justice (adala) of the Companions, which is a fundamental Sunni belief, and cites verses from the Quran and the consensus of scholars to support it. This is important because the reliability of the transmission chain depends on the integrity of the Companions. The passage then explains the necessity of ijtihad: since new events arise constantly, and the texts (Quran and Sunnah) do not cover every detail explicitly, qualified scholars must exert their own reasoning (ijtihad bi al-ra'y). The hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal is presented as a proof for the legitimacy of ijtihad. The text also addresses the authenticity of this hadith, noting that although its chain is weak, scholars have accepted it due to its widespread acceptance and use as evidence. Finally, it describes the highest class of mujtahids, who derive rulings directly from the primary sources without taqlid. This sets the stage for the Hanafi school's claim to be based on sound methodology and continuous transmission.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): The exertion of mental effort by a qualified scholar to derive legal rulings when no clear text exists.
الاجتهاد بالرأي (al-ijtihad bi al-ra'y): Reasoned opinion, as distinct from pure textual deduction. It includes qiyas and istihsan.
المجتهد (mujtahid): A scholar qualified to perform ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Followers and later scholars resort to ijtihad?
A) Because they did not have access to the texts
B) Because the number of new events exceeds what could be covered by the apparent meanings of texts
C) Because they wanted to create new rulings
D) Because they were forced by the rulers

Key Takeaway
Ijtihad is a legitimate source of law in the Hanafi school, supported by the prophetic approval of Mu'adh's reasoning and necessitated by the infinitude of new events.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
The Proof for Ijtihad: The Hadith of Mu'adh
Explaining the need for ijtihad and citing the hadith of Mu'adh as proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three-step method of legal reasoning approved by the Prophet in the hadith of Mu'adh and explain its significance as a proof for qiyas.

Core Idea
The Prophet's approval of Mu'adh's method establishes qiyas as a legitimate source after Quran and Sunnah.

In studying Hanafi usul, we often encounter 'ijtihad bi al-ra'y', sometimes misunderstood as arbitrary opinion. However, the Hanafi tradition grounds this concept firmly in the Prophetic sunnah. The hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal provides the foundational proof for using reasoned opinion in legal deduction. This lesson unpacks that hadith and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the Prophet sent Mu'adh as a judge to Yemen and asked him about his method of judging.
The context is the appointment of a judge.
2
Extract the sequence
Mu'adh's method: first the Book of Allah, then the Sunnah of the Messenger, then ijtihad bi al-ra'y.
This establishes a hierarchy of sources.
3
Analyze the Prophet's response
The Prophet praised Allah for guiding His messenger to what pleases Him, thereby approving Mu'adh's method.
Approval implies legitimacy.
4
Understand the Hanafi interpretation
Hanafi scholars accept this hadith despite chain weakness because of widespread acceptance. They interpret 'ijtihad bi al-ra'y' as disciplined qiyas.
See al-Khatib's defense of the hadith.
5
Connect to usul
This hadith is the primary evidence for qiyas as the fourth source of law in the Hanafi school.
Qiyas is not arbitrary but based on shared effective cause.

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
وكذا أعلام التابعين الذين هم يزاحمونهم في الفتوى ، ووافقوا لهم بغير خلاف، ونقلوا أحكام الدين منهم إلى الأخلاف، محيين سَنَن الأسلاف، حاوين مآثر الأشراف. ولما كانت حوادث الأيام خارجة عن التعداد، ومعرفة أحكامها لازمة إلى يوم التناد، وكانت ظواهر النصوص غير موفية ببيانها ، بل لا بد لها من طريق واف بشأنها اضطروا إلى الاجتهاد بالرأي ، فاجتهدوا وأسَّسُوا قواعد الأصول وشيدوا، فعزموا على تعيين المذهب، ومهدوا مستفيضين بما روي عن رسول الله ل له أَنه لَمَّا بعث معاذه إلى اليمن قاضياً، قال له : «بم تقضي يا معاذ؟ قال : بكتاب الله ، قال : فإن لم تجد؟ قال: بسنة رسوله، قال: فإن لم تجد ؟ قال : اجتهد فيه برأيي، فقال رسول الله : الحمد الله الذي وفق رسول رسوله بما يرضى به رسوله (۱).
ثم إن علماء الدين والأئمة المجتهدين بذلوا جهدهم في تحقيق المسائل الشرعية، وتدقيق النظائر الفرعيّة، واستنبطوا أحكام الفروع عن الأدلة الأربعة، فاتفاقهم حجة قاطعة، واختلافهم رحمة واسعة، فمنهم أصحاب الطبقة العالية من الاجتهاد، وهم الذين صادف الدين بهم أقوى عماد ، وضعوا المسائل على قواعد أصولهم، وهذبوا مسائل الاجتهاد، مع تنقيح طرق النظر على مذاهبهم ، يستمدون في استنباط الأحكام من الكتاب والسنة والإجماع والقياس من غير تقليد في الأصول ولا في الفروع لأحدٍ من الناس، وحالهم متفاوتة في اشتهار مذاهبهم، واعتبار مشاربهم.

(٥) أي قعد القواعد وأصل الأصول بصورة أوسع وأدق وأنظم ممن سبقه ، إذ أن ما عهد عنه من مسائل تنتظم في سلك واحد لأصول معيّنة لا تخرج عنها، فكل الفروع المتشابهة ترجع لقاعدة واحدة، الأمر الذي جعل من بعدهم يقر له بالأحقية في التقليد والاتباع، حتى عد واضع علم
A blazing lamp. 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. Likewise the prominent Followers (tabi'un) who rival them in giving fatwa, and agreed with them without disagreement, and transmitted the rulings of the religion from them to the successors, reviving the practices of the predecessors, encompassing the noble deeds of the nobles. And since the events of the days are beyond enumeration, and knowledge of their rulings is necessary until the Day of Summoning, and the apparent meanings of the texts are insufficient for their elucidation, rather they require a sufficient method for their affairs, they were compelled to exert independent reasoning (ijtihad) with opinion (ra'y). So they exerted ijtihad and established the principles of usul and strengthened them. They resolved to designate the madhhab, and paved the way, spreading the report from the Messenger of Allah that when he sent Mu'adh to Yemen as a judge, he said: 'By what will you judge, O Mu'adh?' He said: 'By the Book of Allah.' He said: 'If you do not find (it)?' He said: 'By the Sunnah of His Messenger.' He said: 'If you do not find?' He said: 'I will exert my opinion (ajtahidu bi-ra'yi).' Then the Messenger of Allah said: 'Praise be to Allah who has guided the messenger of His Messenger to what pleases His Messenger.' (1) Then the scholars of the religion and the mujtahid imams exerted their utmost in verifying the legal issues, scrutinizing the analogous subsidiary matters, and deriving the rulings of branches from the four proofs. Their agreement is a decisive proof, and their disagreement is a vast mercy. Among them are those of the highest level of ijtihad, who are those with whom the religion found its strongest support. They formulated issues on the principles of their usul, and refined the issues of ijtihad, while refining the methods of consideration according to their madhhabs. They derive rulings from the Book, the Sunnah, consensus (ijma), and analogy (qiyas), without taqlid of anyone in usul or furu'. Their condition varies in the fame of their madhhabs and the consideration of their approaches. (1) Recorded by Abu Dawud (3:313) and al-Tirmidhi (3:616) who indicated its weakness, and it has supportive evidence from mawquf narrations of 'Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Ibn 'Abbas. Al-Bayhaqi recorded them in his Sunan (10:114) after this hadith, strengthening it. Likewise in Mirqat al-Su'ud Sharh Sunan Abi Dawud by al-Suyuti. (End of footnote). I say: Al-Khatib said in al-Faqih wa al-Mutafaqqih (1:188): 'The people of knowledge have accepted it and used it as evidence, so we know its validity with them, just as we know the validity of the statement of the Messenger of Allah: 'No bequest to an heir,' and his statement about the sea: 'Its water is pure, its dead are permissible,' and his statement: 'If the two parties to a sale differ over the price and the goods are still present, they take an oath and annul the transaction,' and his statement: 'Blood-money is upon the family of the perpetrator.' Even though these hadiths are not established in terms of chain, since they were received by the general masses from the general masses, they are independent in their validity without seeking a chain. Similarly, the hadith of Mu'adh, since all have used it as evidence, they are independent in its validity without seeking a chain. Its completion is in the margin of al-Hudud wa al-Ahkam al-Fiqhiyyah (pp. 82-83). - The First Study: The Spread of Knowledge. And among those whose madhhabs became widespread in the ages, and whose knowledge became famous in the regions and cities, are our greatest Imam Abu Hanifa Nu'man ibn Thabit al-Kufi, Malik ibn Anas, Sufyan al-Thawri (1), and Ibn Abi Layla Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Rahman (2), and 'Abd al-Rahman al-Awza'i (3), and Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and Dawud ibn 'Ali al-Asfahani (4). But four among them were singled out: Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ibn Hanbal, by guidance. These four broke the norm in the sense of a miracle, by care from Allah. So the fame of their madhhabs in the horizons, and the consideration of their usul and furu' in the leaves of papers, and the consensus of hearts to adopt them over the passage of ages without others, testifies to the purity of their intention and goodness of their innermost selves. Especially the Greatest Imam, the noble ancient hero, the lamp of the nation, the crown of the community, the moon of the imams, Abu Hanifa. Allah has specially favored him, and gathered in his person such virtues as He has not gathered even a sample in anyone else, until his knowledge became widespread, and his madhhab became famous due to the multitude of mujtahids following his madhhab. He manifested the sciences of the Shari'ah among the Muslims, and spread the rulings of branches among the believers. For he was the first to branch out (farra'a) in fiqh (5), and authored and compiled, by the agreement of those who persistently attended his lessons, from... (1) He is Sufyan ibn Sa'id ibn Masruq al-Thawri al-Kufi, Abu 'Abd Allah. Ibn Ma'in said: Sufyan is the Commander of the Faithful in hadith. (95-161 AH). See: Wafayat al-A'yan (2:386-391), Mir'at al-Jinan (1:345-347). (2) He is Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla al-Ansari, Abu 'Abd al-Rahman. Muhammad ibn Yunus said: He was the most learned jurist of his time. He held the judgeship of Kufa and served as judge for thirty-three years, and was a jurist and mufti. (d. 148 AH). See: al-'Ibar (1:211), Mir'at al-Jinan (1:306). (3) He is 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Amr ibn Yahmad al-Awza'i, Abu 'Amr, the imam of the people of Syria. He lived in Beirut, and it is estimated that he was asked about seventy thousand issues, to which he replied. (88-157 AH). See: Wafayat (3:127-128), Mir'at al-Jinan (1:251). (4) He is Dawud ibn 'Ali ibn Khalf al-Asbahani, Abu Sulayman, nicknamed al-Zahiri, so called for taking the literal meaning (zahir) of the Book and Sunnah and avoiding interpretation, opinion, and analogy. He was known as al-Asbahani because his mother was from Isfahan, and he was Iraqi. (201-270 AH). See: al-Mizan (3:26-28), Wafayat (2:255-257), Tabaqat al-Shirazi (p. 102). (5) i.e., he established the rules and originated the principles in a broader, more precise, and more systematic manner than those before him. Because the issues known from him are organized in a single thread according to specific principles from which they do not depart, so all similar branches refer back to a single rule, a fact that led those after him to acknowledge his priority in taqlid and following, until he was counted as the originator of the science.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is the introductory front matter of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah. The author begins by praising the predecessors and then explains the necessity of ijtihad. He cites the well-known hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) approves Mu'adh's method of judging by the Quran, then the Sunnah, then ijtihad bi al-ra'y. This hadith, despite some weakness in chain, is accepted by the scholars as a basis for the validity of qiyas (analogy). The author then discusses the early mujtahid imams, listing them and explaining that their agreement is a decisive proof, while their differences are a mercy. He notes that although many imams existed, only four madhhabs became widespread: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali. He attributes this to divine grace and the purity of their intentions. Special praise is given to Imam Abu Hanifa, highlighting that he was the first to systematize fiqh and its subsidiary branches, attracting many followers. The footnotes provide biographical details and further defense of the hadith's acceptance. The overall purpose is to establish the authority and methodology of Hanafi fiqh within the context of the broader Islamic legal tradition.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد بالرأي (al-ijtihad bi al-ra'y): Exerting independent reasoning using personal opinion, particularly qiyas, when textual sources are silent.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical deduction: extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause ('illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the third source of law in Mu'adh's method according to the hadith cited?
A) Ijma (consensus)
B) Qiyas (analogy)
C) Ijtihad bi al-ra'y (reasoned opinion)
D) Istihsan (juristic preference)

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's approval of Mu'adh's method legitimizes qiyas as a source of law after Quran and Sunnah.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
The Prominence of the Four Imams and the Special Status of Abu Hanifa
Introducing the major mujtahid imams and highlighting the Hanafi school's prominence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four major Imams whose madhhabs became widespread and explain the factors that led to the prominence of the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The four madhhabs gained dominance by divine grace, but Abu Hanifa's systematic methodology made his school the most widespread.

While many mujtahid imams existed in early Islam, history has preserved the teachings of four major schools. Among them, Imam Abu Hanifa holds a special place for being the first to systematically organize fiqh. This lesson explores why these four schools became the standard and what sets Abu Hanifa apart.

Guided Path
1
List the early mujtahid imams mentioned
The text names several imams: Abu Hanifa, Malik, Sufyan al-Thawri, Ibn Abi Layla, al-Awza'i, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and Dawud al-Zahiri.
All were notable scholars.
2
Identify the four that became widespread
Only four are singled out by 'guidance' (al-hidaya): Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
The others did not attain the same level of diffusion.
3
Understand the reason for their prominence
The author attributes it to divine care (karama) and the purity of their intentions, evidenced by the consensus of hearts over time.
It is presented as a miracle ('adam inkhiraq al-'adah).
4
Note the special praise for Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa is described as the 'greatest imam', 'lamp of the nation', etc. He was the first to systematically branch out (farra'a) in fiqh, establishing principles and attracting many followers.
This systematization is key to his school's spread.
5
Reflect on the significance for Hanafi students
Understanding this historical context reinforces the authority and methodology of the Hanafi school.
It encourages students to value the systematic approach.

A blazing lamp. 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. Likewise the prominent Followers (tabi'un) who rival them in giving fatwa, and agreed with them without disagreement, and transmitted the rulings of the religion from them to the successors, reviving the practices of the predecessors, encompassing the noble deeds of the nobles. And since the events of the days are beyond enumeration, and knowledge of their rulings is necessary until the Day of Summoning, and the apparent meanings of the texts are insufficient for their elucidation, rather they require a sufficient method for their affairs, they were compelled to exert independent reasoning (ijtihad) with opinion (ra'y). So they exerted ijtihad and established the principles of usul and strengthened them. They resolved to designate the madhhab, and paved the way, spreading the report from the Messenger of Allah that when he sent Mu'adh to Yemen as a judge, he said: 'By what will you judge, O Mu'adh?' He said: 'By the Book of Allah.' He said: 'If you do not find (it)?' He said: 'By the Sunnah of His Messenger.' He said: 'If you do not find?' He said: 'I will exert my opinion (ajtahidu bi-ra'yi).' Then the Messenger of Allah said: 'Praise be to Allah who has guided the messenger of His Messenger to what pleases His Messenger.' (1) Then the scholars of the religion and the mujtahid imams exerted their utmost in verifying the legal issues, scrutinizing the analogous subsidiary matters, and deriving the rulings of branches from the four proofs. Their agreement is a decisive proof, and their disagreement is a vast mercy. Among them are those of the highest level of ijtihad, who are those with whom the religion found its strongest support. They formulated issues on the principles of their usul, and refined the issues of ijtihad, while refining the methods of consideration according to their madhhabs. They derive rulings from the Book, the Sunnah, consensus (ijma), and analogy (qiyas), without taqlid of anyone in usul or furu'. Their condition varies in the fame of their madhhabs and the consideration of their approaches. (1) Recorded by Abu Dawud (3:313) and al-Tirmidhi (3:616) who indicated its weakness, and it has supportive evidence from mawquf narrations of 'Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Ibn 'Abbas. Al-Bayhaqi recorded them in his Sunan (10:114) after this hadith, strengthening it. Likewise in Mirqat al-Su'ud Sharh Sunan Abi Dawud by al-Suyuti. (End of footnote). I say: Al-Khatib said in al-Faqih wa al-Mutafaqqih (1:188): 'The people of knowledge have accepted it and used it as evidence, so we know its validity with them, just as we know the validity of the statement of the Messenger of Allah: 'No bequest to an heir,' and his statement about the sea: 'Its water is pure, its dead are permissible,' and his statement: 'If the two parties to a sale differ over the price and the goods are still present, they take an oath and annul the transaction,' and his statement: 'Blood-money is upon the family of the perpetrator.' Even though these hadiths are not established in terms of chain, since they were received by the general masses from the general masses, they are independent in their validity without seeking a chain. Similarly, the hadith of Mu'adh, since all have used it as evidence, they are independent in its validity without seeking a chain. Its completion is in the margin of al-Hudud wa al-Ahkam al-Fiqhiyyah (pp. 82-83). - The First Study: The Spread of Knowledge. And among those whose madhhabs became widespread in the ages, and whose knowledge became famous in the regions and cities, are our greatest Imam Abu Hanifa Nu'man ibn Thabit al-Kufi, Malik ibn Anas, Sufyan al-Thawri (1), and Ibn Abi Layla Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Rahman (2), and 'Abd al-Rahman al-Awza'i (3), and Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and Dawud ibn 'Ali al-Asfahani (4). But four among them were singled out: Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ibn Hanbal, by guidance. These four broke the norm in the sense of a miracle, by care from Allah. So the fame of their madhhabs in the horizons, and the consideration of their usul and furu' in the leaves of papers, and the consensus of hearts to adopt them over the passage of ages without others, testifies to the purity of their intention and goodness of their innermost selves. Especially the Greatest Imam, the noble ancient hero, the lamp of the nation, the crown of the community, the moon of the imams, Abu Hanifa. Allah has specially favored him, and gathered in his person such virtues as He has not gathered even a sample in anyone else, until his knowledge became widespread, and his madhhab became famous due to the multitude of mujtahids following his madhhab. He manifested the sciences of the Shari'ah among the Muslims, and spread the rulings of branches among the believers. For he was the first to branch out (farra'a) in fiqh (5), and authored and compiled, by the agreement of those who persistently attended his lessons, from... (1) He is Sufyan ibn Sa'id ibn Masruq al-Thawri al-Kufi, Abu 'Abd Allah. Ibn Ma'in said: Sufyan is the Commander of the Faithful in hadith. (95-161 AH). See: Wafayat al-A'yan (2:386-391), Mir'at al-Jinan (1:345-347). (2) He is Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla al-Ansari, Abu 'Abd al-Rahman. Muhammad ibn Yunus said: He was the most learned jurist of his time. He held the judgeship of Kufa and served as judge for thirty-three years, and was a jurist and mufti. (d. 148 AH). See: al-'Ibar (1:211), Mir'at al-Jinan (1:306). (3) He is 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Amr ibn Yahmad al-Awza'i, Abu 'Amr, the imam of the people of Syria. He lived in Beirut, and it is estimated that he was asked about seventy thousand issues, to which he replied. (88-157 AH). See: Wafayat (3:127-128), Mir'at al-Jinan (1:251). (4) He is Dawud ibn 'Ali ibn Khalf al-Asbahani, Abu Sulayman, nicknamed al-Zahiri, so called for taking the literal meaning (zahir) of the Book and Sunnah and avoiding interpretation, opinion, and analogy. He was known as al-Asbahani because his mother was from Isfahan, and he was Iraqi. (201-270 AH). See: al-Mizan (3:26-28), Wafayat (2:255-257), Tabaqat al-Shirazi (p. 102). (5) i.e., he established the rules and originated the principles in a broader, more precise, and more systematic manner than those before him. Because the issues known from him are organized in a single thread according to specific principles from which they do not depart, so all similar branches refer back to a single rule, a fact that led those after him to acknowledge his priority in taqlid and following, until he was counted as the originator of the science.

Commentary
This passage is the introductory front matter of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah. The author begins by praising the predecessors and then explains the necessity of ijtihad. He cites the well-known hadith of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) approves Mu'adh's method of judging by the Quran, then the Sunnah, then ijtihad bi al-ra'y. This hadith, despite some weakness in chain, is accepted by the scholars as a basis for the validity of qiyas (analogy). The author then discusses the early mujtahid imams, listing them and explaining that their agreement is a decisive proof, while their differences are a mercy. He notes that although many imams existed, only four madhhabs became widespread: Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali. He attributes this to divine grace and the purity of their intentions. Special praise is given to Imam Abu Hanifa, highlighting that he was the first to systematize fiqh and its subsidiary branches, attracting many followers. The footnotes provide biographical details and further defense of the hadith's acceptance. The overall purpose is to establish the authority and methodology of Hanafi fiqh within the context of the broader Islamic legal tradition.

Key Terms
المذهب (al-madhhab): A school of jurisprudence, the collection of rulings and principles attributed to a particular imam.
التابعون (al-tabi'un): The generation after the Companions of the Prophet.
أبو حنيفة (Abu Hanifa): The founder of the Hanafi school, known for his use of qiyas and systematic approach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Malik ibn Anas
B) Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i
C) Abu Hanifa
D) Ahmad ibn Hanbal

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa is considered the founder of systematic fiqh; his methodology attracted many followers and led to the widespread adoption of his school.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
The Spread of Knowledge and Imam Abu Hanifa's Legacy
Biographical introduction praising Abu Hanifa and his madhhab's prominence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Abu Hanifa is considered the first to systematize fiqh and identify the factors that led to the widespread acceptance of the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's method of branching out (tafri') made fiqh systematic and accessible, securing the Hanafi school's global spread.

This lesson introduces a passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah Sharh al-Wiqayah, a commentary on a Hanafi fiqh text. The passage is a biographical preface that celebrates the spread of the Hanafi school and the unique virtues of its founder, Imam Abu Hanifa. You will learn how the author describes the divine favor behind the prominence of the four schools and the specific contributions of Abu Hanifa that earned him the title 'the Greatest Imam' and the praise of even his rival, Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scholars Listed
Read the passage and list the early imams whose schools became widespread. Then note which four are singled out as having 'broken the norm' through divine guidance.
This sets the stage for understanding the Hanafi school's position among others.
2
Analyze the Concept of 'Shuyu' al-Ilm'
Consider what the author means by the spread of knowledge (shuyu' al-ilm). Why does he consider the agreement of hearts over generations a miracle (karamah)?
This helps you grasp the theological and historical weight of the Hanafi school's acceptance.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's Pioneering Role
Focus on the phrase 'فإنه أوّل مَن فَرَّعَ في الفقه' (he was the first to branch out in fiqh). Understand what 'farra'a' means in the context of usul al-fiqh: deriving subsidiary rulings from principles using hypothetical cases (al-fiqh al-taqdiri).
This is the core claim of Abu Hanifa's primacy in legal theory.
4
Recognize the Circle of Students
The passage lists notable students (e.g., Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar). Why does the author emphasize their agreement and presence?
This demonstrates that the Hanafi school was built collectively by a generation of mujtahids.
5
Reflect on the Endorsement by al-Shafi'i
The passage ends with al-Shafi'i's famous statement: 'People are dependent on Abu Hanifa in fiqh.' Analyze its significance as a cross-madhhab validation.
This serves as a powerful testimony to Abu Hanifa's foundational role.

The First Study: The Spread of Knowledge. And among those whose school spread in the ages and whose knowledge became famous in regions and cities is our greatest Imam Abu Hanifa Nu'man bin Thabit al-Kufi, Malik bin Anas, Sufyan al-Thawri (1), Ibn Abi Layla Muhammad bin Abd al-Rahman (2), Abd al-Rahman al-Awza'i (3), Muhammad bin Idris al-Shafi'i, Ahmad bin Hanbal, and Dawud bin Ali al-Isfahani (4). But singled out from among them were the four: Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ibn Hanbal by divine guidance. These four broke the norm in the sense of a miracle from Allah, so their schools became famous across the horizons, and their principles and branches were considered in the pages of papers. The hearts' agreement to take them over others throughout the ages testifies to the sincerity of their intention and their good inner state. Especially the Greatest Imam, the noble elder, the lamp of the nation, the crown of the community, the moon of the imams, Abu Hanifa: Allah singled him out with His care and gathered in his person such virtues as He did not gather in anyone else, until his knowledge spread and his school became famous due to the large number of mujtahids following his school. He made the sciences of Shari'ah manifest among Muslims and spread the rulings of branches among the believers. For he was the first to branch out in fiqh (5) and to compose and author, by the agreement of those who attended his lessons from among the famous mujtahid scholars, and the gathering of his various circles to his assembly from the multitude of eminent predecessors, such as Abu Yusuf, preeminent in hadith and language; Muhammad, preeminent in fiqh, grammar, and eloquence; Zufar, the intelligent jurist in qiyas; Hasan bin Ziyad, preeminent in questioning and branching; Abdullah bin al-Mubarak (1), correct in his opinion; Waki' bin al-Jarrah (2), the ascetic exegete; Hafs bin Ghiyath bin Talq (3), the intelligent and clever in judging between people; Yahya bin Zakariyya bin Abi Za'ida in hadith and mastery of branches; Asad bin Amr (5), the judge; and Nuh bin Abi... (footnotes continue with biographical details).
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a biographical front matter from the book 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah Sharh al-Wiqayah. It opens the first study on the spread of knowledge. The author lists the early imams whose schools became widespread: Abu Hanifa, Malik, Sufyan al-Thawri, Ibn Abi Layla, al-Awza'i, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and Dawud al-Zahiri. However, he notes that only four—Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad—were singled out for divine guidance such that their schools became universally accepted. This is presented as a miracle (karamah) attesting to their sincerity. The author then focuses on Imam Abu Hanifa, praising him with superlative titles and highlighting that Allah gathered in him virtues not found in others. The passage emphasizes that Abu Hanifa was the first to branch out in fiqh (fara'a fi al-fiqh), meaning he systematized the derivation of branches from principles. This is supported by the agreement of his students—renowned mujtahids like Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, and others—who attended his lessons. The footnote explains that 'branching out' means he laid down rules and principles in a broader, more precise, and more organized manner than those before him, so that similar branches all trace back to a single base. It also mentions that Abu Hanifa is credited with laying the foundation of hypothetical fiqh (al-fiqh al-taqdiri) and is reported to have formulated 83,000 issues. The passage concludes with a quote from Imam al-Shafi'i: 'People are dependent on Abu Hanifa in fiqh.' This is a strong endorsement of Abu Hanifa's foundational role in Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
شيوع العلم (shuyu' al-‘ilm): The widespread dissemination of knowledge, specifically the acceptance and propagation of certain legal schools.
الإمام الأعظم (al-Imam al-A‘zam): Title for Abu Hanifa, meaning 'the Greatest Imam,' reflecting his preeminence.
فَرَّعَ في الفقه (farra‘a fi al-fiqh): To branch out in fiqh, i.e., to systematically derive subsidiary rulings from foundational principles.
الفقه التقديري (al-fiqh al-taqdiri): Hypothetical fiqh: the method of assuming possible scenarios to test and establish legal principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is described as 'the first to branch out in fiqh'?
A) Imam Malik
B) Imam Abu Hanifa
C) Imam al-Shafi'i
D) Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa is credited with pioneering the systematic derivation of fiqh branches from principles, which ensured the Hanafi school's enduring spread and acceptance.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Spread of Hanafi Fiqh and the Mongol Invasions: A Historical Perspective
Historical account of Hanafi school spread and destruction by Mongols

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the geographic spread of Hanafi fiqh before the Mongol invasions and explain the impact of the Mongol invasions on the Hanafi scholarly community.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh spread from Iraq to India and beyond, but Mongol invasions devastated its centers, forcing scholars to migrate and adapt.

This passage is not a legal ruling but a historical narrative that sets the stage for the development of the Hanafi school. It shows how the school grew through the efforts of its early scholars, earning praise from other imams, and how its prosperity was shattered by the Mongol conquests. Understanding this history helps you appreciate the resilience of the Hanafi tradition and the context in which many classical texts were written.

Guided Path
1
Identify the praise from other imams
Read what Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i said about Abu Hanifa. This establishes the high regard for the school's founder.
2
Map the geographical spread
List the regions and cities mentioned: Iraq, Khurasan, Transoxiana, India, etc. Note the vast area covered.
3
Describe the scholarly activities
Identify what Hanafi scholars did: teaching, writing, issuing fatwas on nawazil, compiling issues.
4
Analyze the Mongol impact
Note the description of Genghis Khan and Hulagu's actions: killing, destruction, and the specific events in Khwarazm and Baghdad.
Pay attention to the dates (616 AH, 656 AH).
5
Trace the migration
Where did the Hanafi jurists go? They moved to Damascus and Aleppo, and later due to Circassian oppression, they migrated again.
