Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
10 min
Condition of the Mufti: Knowledge of Evidence
Methodological discussion on fatwa conditions and madhhab authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the early Hanafi condition for issuing fatwa (knowledge of evidence), explain the later relaxation by Ibn Nujaym, and identify the authoritative sources for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The condition for fatwa evolved from requiring direct knowledge of evidence to relying on the madhhab's verified rulings.

This passage addresses a foundational methodological issue: what qualifies a mufti to issue a fatwa? The early Hanafi school insisted that a mufti must know the specific evidence (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, or Qiyas) from which the ruling was derived. Later jurists, recognizing the difficulty for later generations, relaxed this requirement to suffice with memorizing the madhhab's established rulings. The passage also defends the school against critics who misuse Imam al-Shafi'i's statement to undermine the authority of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source principle for fatwa
The first line states that a ruling's source must be from the Book, Sunnah, Consensus, or clear analogy (qiyas jali). This is the foundational principle for Hanafi fatwa.
This aligns with the Hanafi usul that qiyas jali is a valid source when explicit texts are absent.
2
Understand the early Hanafi condition
The narrative of the four companions of Abu Hanifa (Zufar, Abu Yusuf, 'Afiyah, and another) shows their consensus that it is not permissible to give fatwa without knowing the source of the Imam's ruling.
This consensus (ijma) is a strong evidence for the early position.
3
Note the later relaxation by Ibn Nujaym
Ibn Nujaym in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states that the condition of knowing the evidence was for the early generations; in later times, it suffices to memorize the school's rulings (hifz).
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position for fatwa in later times.
4
Analyze the use of al-Shafi'i's statement
The passage quotes al-Shafi'i's well-known instruction to follow authentic hadith over his own opinion. The author explains that this was meant as praise for al-Shafi'i's humility, not as a license for laymen to reject school rulings.
This section defends the madhhab against contemporary critics who misuse the quote.
5
Recognize the hierarchy of authority in the madhhab
The passage concludes by explaining that the relied-upon positions are determined by the mujtahids within the school, not by arbitrary use of hadith. Only the four recognized imams have the authority to derive rulings directly from primary sources.
This underscores the importance of following the madhhab's refined positions.

(۳) أقول : إن ما تفضل به ملا علي القاري غير مسلّم على إطلاقه ؛ لأن المعتمد في المذاهب ما حرره ونقحه المجتهدون فيها وفقاً لأسس وقواعد معتمدة لديهم تبين الصحيح الراجح فيما ذهب إليه الإمام المجتهد المطلق للمذهب ، فانظر رحمك الله إلى هذه الدقة العالية التي ساروا عليها في بيان حكم الله في كل مسألة، فاستخراج المسائل من الكتاب والسنة لم يسلّموا فيها لأي أحد، وإنما ركنوا فيها إلى الأئمة الكبار الذين شهدت لهم الأمة بالعلم والورع ورزقهم الله القبول. فمن بين العديد من المجتهدين المستقلين في أمتنا لم يسلّم إلا للأئمة الأربعة، وأخذت الأحكام المستخرجة من الكتاب والسنة من أفواههم، فهم فرسان هذا العلم الذين سبروا ونظروا وقعدوا وأسسوا وهم الذين بلغوا الدرجة القصوى في الفقه والحديث ، حفاظ هذه الأمة عند كل عالم منصف. قال إمام الحرمين: «أجمع المحققون على أن العوام ليس لهم أن يتعلقوا بمذاهب الصحابة بل عليهم أن يتَّبعوا مذاهب الأئمة الذين سبروا ونظروا وبوبوا ؛ لأن الصحابة لم يعتنوا بتهذيب المسائل والاجتهاد وإيضاح طرق النظر بخلاف من بعدهم» كما في «مواهب الجليل»
.(۳۰ : ۱)
فعدوا من
ومع ذلك جاء بعـدهـم آلاف العلماء المجتهدون في مذاهبهم فحرروا أقوالهم وحققوا آراءهم، فصار المعتمد عند العلماء في الفتوى والعمل ما رجّحه وصححه طبقة المجتهدين في المذهب، فهذه مرحلة أخرى بعد مرحلة الأئمة المجتهدين المستقلين، وكل ذلك لدقة هذا المقام وصعوبته ؛ لأنه بيان لحكم الله فلا يجرؤ عليه إلا شقي خاسر. إذا تمهد لك ذلك، تبين حال مسائل الفقه في المذاهب الفقهية المعتمدة من التنقيح والتحقيق
Its source is from the Book, the Sunnah, or the Consensus of the Ummah, and the clear analogy (qiyas jali) in the matter. And al-Shafi'i said: 'If a hadith is authentic contradicting my statement, then throw my statement against the wall and act upon the authentic hadith.' Likewise. If you know this, then know that if the Imam had no text regarding the objective, then it would be incumbent upon his followers among the noble scholars, let alone the common people, to act upon what is authentically established from the Messenger of Allah. If the Imam negates the pointing (isara) and it is established from the 'owner of the glad tidings' (the Prophet), then there is no doubt in preferring the one who affirms, traced to the Messenger of Allah. How much more so when his explicit transmission matches what is established from the Messenger of Allah with authentic chain. So whoever is fair and not obstinate knows that this is the way of the religious people among the predecessors and successors. Whoever deviates from that is destroyed, described as a stubborn opponent. Even if he were among the eminent people. End quote. And in another treatise of his on the topic of the gesture (isara) called 'al-Tadhin li al-Tazyin': 'The one who says that the fatwa is upon leaving the gesture claims to be a mujtahid in the matter. That applies when there are two narrations from the Imam, or one from him and another from his two companions, and he needs evidence for preference since preference without a preponderant factor is not acceptable, nor correction without a corrector. If there are two narrations, the preponderant is what agrees with the prophetic hadiths and matches the statements of the majority of the scholars of the Imams, even if it opposes another statement from among the reliable masters who say that the fatwa is upon the gesture and that there is no dispute that it is from the Sunnah.' End quote. I say: What Mulla 'Ali al-Qari favored is not absolutely conceded because the relied upon in the schools is what the mujtahids within them edited and refined according to established principles and rules that clarify the correct and preponderant in what the absolute mujtahid Imam of the school held. So look, may Allah have mercy on you, at this high precision they followed in explaining Allah's ruling in every issue. Extracting issues from the Book and Sunnah was not entrusted to just anyone; rather they relied on the great Imams whom the Ummah testified to have knowledge and piety and whom Allah granted acceptance. Among the many independent mujtahids in our Ummah, only the four Imams were accepted, and rulings extracted from the Book and Sunnah were taken from their mouths. They were the knights of this knowledge who probed, examined, established principles, and founded; they reached the highest degree in fiqh and hadith, the preservers of this Ummah according to every fair scholar. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'The verifiers have agreed that the common people have no right to cling to the schools of the Companions, but rather they must follow the schools of the Imams who probed, examined, and categorized; because the Companions did not concern themselves with refining issues, ijtihad, and clarifying methods of reasoning, unlike those after them.' As in 'Mawahib al-Jalil'. Then after them came thousands of mujtahid scholars within their schools who edited their statements and verified their opinions. So what became relied upon among scholars for fatwa and practice is what the class of mujtahids within the school deemed preponderant and sound. This is another stage after the stage of the independent mujtahid Imams. All this is due to the precision and difficulty of this station because it is an explanation of Allah's ruling; none dares to undertake it except a wretched loser. When this becomes clear to you, the state of fiqh issues in the relied-upon schools of fiqh becomes evident in terms of refinement and verification.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion on the authority of the Hanafi school and the conditions for issuing fatwa. It begins by citing a principle attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa: that the source of a ruling must be from the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, or clear analogy. Then it quotes Imam al-Shafi'i's well-known statement about following authentic hadith over his own opinion, but the author clarifies that this statement is used by some to attack the schools, while it was actually meant as praise for al-Shafi'i's humility. The author then argues that if Imam Abu Hanifa had no explicit text on a matter, his followers among scholars and laymen must act upon what is authentically reported from the Prophet, even if the Imam's own opinion differs. The passage then discusses the specific issue of the gesture (isara) in prayer, where some say the fatwa is against it, but the author cites a treatise that says preference should be given to what agrees with hadith. Finally, the author (likely the commentator) critiques Mulla 'Ali al-Qari's position, asserting that the relied-upon positions in the madhhab are determined by the mujtahids within the school, not by anyone arbitrarily. The common people must follow the mujtahid imams, not directly interpret the primary sources. The passage emphasizes the rigor and precision of the Hanafi school in deriving rulings, and that only the four recognized imams have this authority. The commentary follows the order of the text, explaining each part.

Key Terms
القياس الجلي (al-qiyas al-jali): Clear analogy where the effective cause ('illa) is obvious and explicitly mentioned in the primary text.
الحديث الضابط (al-hadith al-dabit): A hadith whose narrators are precise (dabit) and reliable, making it authentic.
الإشارة (al-ishara): The gesture of the finger during tashahhud in prayer; the issue is whether it is sunnah or not.
الحفظ (al-hifz): Memorization of the madhhab's rulings, considered sufficient for giving fatwa according to later Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the consensus of the four companions of Abu Hanifa regarding giving fatwa?
A) It is permissible to give fatwa by simply knowing the Imam's ruling.
B) It is not permissible to give fatwa unless one knows the evidence from which the Imam derived the ruling.
C) It is permissible to give fatwa based on hadith even if it contradicts the Imam.
D) It is obligatory to give fatwa even without knowing the source.

Key Takeaway
The condition for issuing fatwa in the Hanafi school evolved from requiring direct knowledge of evidence (early view) to relying on memorization of the school's established rulings (later mufta bihi), while always maintaining the authority of the mujtahid imams.

Reading Authorial Signals: 'قالوا' and 'عامة المشايخ' in Hanafi Fiqh Works
Explaining how authorial phrases indicate strength or weakness of opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and interpret the phrases 'قالوا' and 'ذهب إليه عامة المشايخ' in Hanafi fiqh texts to determine the strength of an opinion.

Core Idea
'قالوا' signals weakness; 'عامة المشايخ' signals strength — authorial conventions reveal tarjīḥ.

When reading classical Hanafi fiqh works, authors often embed subtle signals about the reliability of opinions. Two key phrases are 'قالوا' (they said) and 'ذهب إليه عامة المشايخ' (the general mashayikh went to it). This lesson unpacks these signals, based on insights from Ibn al-Humam and al-Taftazani, so you can evaluate transmitted opinions with greater precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase
Scan the passage for 'قالوا' or 'ذهب إليه عامة المشايخ' (or its variants).
These appear after presenting an opinion.
2
Interpret the signal
If the phrase is 'قالوا', treat the opinion as weak (ḍaʿīf) according to the custom of al-Hidayah. If it is 'ذهب إليه عامة المشايخ', treat it as strong and relied upon.
Al-Taftazani confirms that 'قالوا' implies weakness.
3
Apply the rule
When you encounter an opinion introduced by 'قالوا', do not take it as muftā bihī unless there is further tarjīḥ. Opinions introduced by 'عامة المشايخ' are reliable and reflect the preferred view.
This rule helps in navigating differing transmissions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر ابن الهمام في «فتح القدير» في «باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة» من «كتاب الصوم»: «إنّ عادته؛ أي صاحب «الهداية» في مثله إفادة الضعف مع الخلاف». انتهى. وكذا ذكره سعد الدين التَّفْتَازَانِيُّ في حواشي الكشاف عند تفسير قوله: حَتَّى يَتَبَيَّنَ لَكُم الخيط الأبيض: «إنّ في لفظ: قالوا؛ إشارة إلى ضعف ما قالوا». فائدة. المراد بقولهم: ذهب إليه عامة المشايخ، ونحوه: أكثرهم. كذا في «فتح القدير» في «باب إدراك الجماعة».
And Ibn al-Humam mentioned in 'Fath al-Qadir' in 'The chapter on what necessitates qada' and kaffarah' from 'The Book of Fasting': 'His habit (i.e., the author of al-Hidayah) in such contexts is to indicate weakness along with disagreement.' End. Likewise, Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani mentioned in his glosses on al-Kashshaf when interpreting His statement 'until the white thread becomes distinct to you': 'Indeed, the expression 'they said' indicates the weakness of what they said.' Benefit: The meaning of their statement 'the general mashayikh went to it' and similar 'most of them' is as in 'Fath al-Qadir' in the chapter on attaining the congregation.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a front-matter lesson on understanding authorial signals in Hanafi fiqh works. It provides two key insights: First, when a text says 'they said' (قالوا), it often implies that the opinion is weak, as per the habit of the author of al-Hidayah and confirmed by al-Taftazani. Second, the phrase 'the general mashayikh went to it' (ذهب إليه عامة المشايخ) or 'most of them' indicates that the view is widely accepted and thus stronger. This lesson helps students interpret the nuances of fiqh texts and recognize the author's implicit evaluation of different opinions.

Key Terms
قالوا (qālū): An authorial phrase meaning 'they said', which in Hanafi fiqh works often signals that the opinion is weak or not relied upon.
ذهب إليه عامة المشايخ (dhahaba ilayhi ʿāmmat al-mashāyikh): A phrase meaning 'the general mashayikh hold this view', indicating a widely accepted and strong opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The opinion is strong and reliable.
C) The opinion is based on disagreement.
D) The opinion is a personal view.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'قالوا' signals weakness; 'ذهب إليه عامة المشايخ' signals strength and tarjīḥ.

Distinguishing 'Valid' from 'Permissible': The Two Meanings of يجوز in Hanafi Texts
Clarifying the contextual meanings of the term 'يجوز' in Hanafi works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of the term 'يجوز' in Hanafi fiqh texts and apply contextual reading to determine whether it indicates validity or permissibility.

Core Idea
'يجوز' can mean 'it is valid' (صح) or 'it is permissible' (حل); context determines which.

When reading Hanafi fiqh works, the word 'يجوز' appears frequently. You might assume it always means 'it is permissible', but careful study shows it often means 'it is valid' (صحيح). This lesson clarifies this critical distinction, drawing on classical commentaries. Understanding this linguistic nuance prevents misinterpretation of rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings
From the passage, note that 'يجوز' can mean 'it is valid' (yaṣiḥḥu) or 'it is permissible' (yaḥillu). The first pertains to legal soundness (ṣiḥḥa) opposed to nullity; the second to being halal, opposed to prohibition.
This duality is explicitly stated in the opening sentence.
2
See examples of contextual usage
The passage mentions that jurists apply 'جاز' to disliked prayers, meaning the prayer is valid but disliked. They do not intend permissibility or negation of dislike. Commentators often clarify 'jāza' as 'even if disliked'.
This is a key example: the act is valid despite being disliked.
3
Understand the broad scope of jawāz
The quote from 'Ḥalbat al-Mujallī' defines jawāz as 'what is not prohibited by law', encompassing permissible, disliked, recommended, and obligatory acts. This shows that jawāz can cover a wide spectrum beyond mere ibāḥa.
Jawāz in this broad sense is the opposite of prohibition.
4
Apply contextual reading from al-Shurunbulālī
The final quote discusses a judge's ruling on an absent party. The ruling is enforceable (nāfidh) but not necessarily permissible (ḥalāl) for the judge. Thus, 'يجوز' in that context means 'is enforceable' or 'is valid', not 'is halal'.
This demonstrates that enforceability does not imply permissibility.
5
Practice with a scenario
Test your understanding by reading a new statement and deciding which meaning applies based on context clues such as the presence of 'مع الكراهة' or the subject matter.
Use the practice tasks to reinforce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدراسة الخامسة: فوائد من يطالع الكتب الفقهية. فائدة: يجوز؛ قد يقال بمعنى: يصح، وقد يقال بمعنى: يحلّ. كذا في «شرح المهذب» للنووي؛ ولذا تراهم يطلقون على الصلاة المكروهة ونحوها: جاز ذلك أو صح ذلك، ويريدون به نفس الصحة المقابل للبطلان من غير القصدِ إلى الإباحة أو نفى الكراهة، ولهذا فسَّرَ الشرَّاح والمحشون كثيراً قولهم: جاز وصح؛ بقولهم: أي مع الكراهة، كما لا يخفى على وسيع النظر. وقال في «حَلْبَة المجلي شرح منية المصلي»: «إنه؛ أي الجواز قد يطلق ويراد به ما لا يمتنع شرعاً، وهو يشمل المباح والمكروه والمندوب والواجب». انتهى. وفي «العقد الفريد لبيان الراجح من جواز التقليد للشَّرنبلالي عند البحث عن بعض عبارات منية «المفتي»: «أو نقول: يجوز بمعنى: يحلّ، فإنّه لا يلزم من النفاذ الحل، فإن الحكم على الغائب نافذ عند شمس الأئمة وغيره، كما ذكره «العمادي».
The Fifth Study: Benefits for those who peruse fiqh books. Benefit: The word 'yajūz' may be used to mean 'it is valid' (yaṣiḥḥu) and may be used to mean 'it is permissible' (yaḥillu). Thus it is stated in 'Sharḥ al-Muhadhdhab' by al-Nawawi. For this reason, you see them applying the expression 'that is permissible' or 'that is valid' to disliked prayers and the like, intending the same validity as opposed to nullity, without aiming at permissibility or negation of dislike. Hence the commentators and glossators frequently interpret their statement 'jāza' and 'ṣaḥḥa' as meaning 'even if disliked', as is not hidden from one with extensive insight. And it is said in 'Ḥalbat al-Mujallī Sharḥ Munyat al-Muṣallī': 'The term jawāz is sometimes used to mean what is not prohibited by law, and it includes the permissible, the disliked, the recommended, and the obligatory.' End. And in 'al-ʿIqd al-Farīd li-Bayān al-Rājiḥ min Jawāz al-Taqlīd' by al-Shurunbulālī, when researching some expressions of 'Munyat al-Muftī': 'Or we say that yajūz means it is permissible, for enforceability does not necessitate permissibility, since a ruling against an absent party is enforceable according to Shams al-A'imma and others, as al-ʿImādī mentioned.'
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a front-matter lesson on the technical usage of the term 'يجوز' (yajūz) in Hanafi fiqh literature. It explains that 'يجوز' can carry two distinct meanings: (1) 'it is valid' (yaṣiḥḥu), referring to legal soundness (ṣiḥḥa) that is the opposite of invalidity (buṭlān), without necessarily implying permissibility or absence of dislike; (2) 'it is permissible' (yaḥillu), indicating that the act is not prohibited. The passage highlights that when jurists say 'jāza' for a disliked prayer, they mean it is valid but disliked. It also notes that 'jawāz' can encompass all categories from permissible to obligatory. The final quote from al-Shurunbulālī adds that a judge's ruling against an absent party is enforceable (nāfidh) even though it may not be legally permissible (ḥalāl) for the judge to rule without evidence, thus demonstrating that 'يجوز' in the sense of 'enforceable' does not equate to 'permissible'. This lesson trains the student to read the term contextually.

Key Terms
يجوز (yajūz): A term used in fiqh that can mean either 'it is valid' (ṣaḥīḥ) as opposed to invalid, or 'it is permissible' (ḥalāl) as opposed to prohibited; context determines the intended meaning.
الجواز (al-jawāz): A term sometimes used broadly to mean 'that which is not legally prohibited', encompassing permissible, disliked, recommended, and obligatory acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is permissible and recommended.
B) The prayer is valid but disliked.
C) The prayer is invalid and impermissible.
D) The prayer is enforceable in court.

Key Takeaway
The term 'يجوز' in Hanafi texts is ambiguous; it can mean 'it is valid' (ṣaḥīḥ) or 'it is permissible' (ḥalāl), and the correct meaning must be inferred from context.

Hanafi Scholarly Periods: Salaf, Khalaf, Muta'akhkhirun
Defines scholarly periods with boundary dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three periods of Hanafi scholars (salaf, khalaf, muta'akhkhirun) and explain the boundary disputes between definitions.

Core Idea
Hanafi works classify scholars into three eras; knowing which era determines the weight of an opinion.

When reading Hanafi fiqh works, you will often encounter terms like al-Salaf, al-Khalaf, and al-Muta'akhkhirun. These are not merely chronological labels; they carry implications about the authority and reliability of the opinions. This lesson unpacks the standard definitions, the contested boundary lines, and how later texts sometimes use these terms inconsistently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the primary definitions for al-Salaf (Abu Hanifa to Muhammad), al-Khalaf (Muhammad to al-Halwani), and al-Muta'akhkhirun (al-Halwani to Hafiz al-Din al-Bukhari) as given in the source.
According to Jami' al-Ulum quoting al-Khiyalat al-Latifa.
2
Note alternative boundaries
Al-Dhahabi places the dividing line between early and later scholars at the year 300 AH, which does not exactly match the Hanafi classification.
Mizan al-I'tidal: 'the dividing line is 300'.
3
Analyse the contradiction
Abd al-Nabi points out that in al-Hidayah, 'some Muta'akhkhirun' includes scholars before al-Halwani, like al-Jurjani (d. ~398 AH) and al-Rustughfani (student of al-Maturidi, d. 333 AH).
This shows the term is used flexibly.
4
Verify with examples
Check the death dates and affiliations of al-Jurjani and al-Rustughfani to confirm they are earlier than al-Halwani (5th century).
Al-Jurjani d. 398 AH, al-Rustughfani d. after 333 AH.
5
Conclude about contextual reading
The boundaries are not rigid; always check the context in which 'muta'akhkhirun' is used.
This is a key skill for reading Hanafi texts.

(3) He is Shams al-Din Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Dhahabi... (4) End from al-Mizan 1:115. (5) From al-Hidayah 1:129. (6) Possibly: Ibrahim ibn Isma'il... (7) From al-Inayah Sharh al-Hidayah 2:287. (8) Kata'ib A'lam al-Akhyar f.128a. [Page 84] ... And likewise al-Rustughfani: with damm on the ra, damm on the ta, between them a sukun on the sin, sukun on the ghayn, and fatha on the fa, relative to Rustughfan a village in Samarkand, his name is Ali ibn Sa'id, preceded al-Halwani, for al-Rustughfani is from the students of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who died in the year 333 [AH]. And Allah knows best. (1) Benefit: Al-Hasan; when mentioned absolutely in our companions' books, what is meant is Ibn Ziyad, the student of Abu Hanifa, and when mentioned absolutely in tafsir books, what is meant is al-Hasan al-Basri. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan (2) in the chapter on nafaqat, quoting from his teacher Burhan al-Din al-Khwarizmi. Benefit: What is meant by al-Imam and al-Imam al-A'zam in our companions' books: he is the founder of the madhhab, Abu Hanifa, and he is meant by their saying: Sahib al-Madhhab. And what is meant by al-Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. And by al-Shaykhayn: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And by al-Tarafayn: Muhammad and Abu Hanifa. (3) And by al-Imam al-Thani: Abu Yusuf. And by al-Imam al-Rabbani: Muhammad. And by their saying: 'when our three imams' it is: Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf. And by al-A'immah al-Arba'ah: Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad; the founders of the well-known schools. Benefit: Shams al-A'imma; when used absolutely in our companions' books, what is meant is Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a collection of definitions (fawa'id) clarifying technical terms used in Hanafi fiqh literature. It begins by classifying Hanafi scholars into three periods: al-Salaf (from Abu Hanifa to Muhammad al-Shaybani), al-Khalaf (from Muhammad al-Shaybani to Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani), and al-Muta'akhkhirun (from al-Halwani to Hafiz al-Din al-Bukhari). Al-Dhahabi is cited as setting the year 300 AH as the boundary between early and later scholars, but a contradiction is noted: in al-Hidayah, 'some Muta'akhkhirun' includes scholars before al-Halwani like al-Jurjani and al-Rustughfani, showing that usage can vary. Biographical details for al-Jurjani and al-Rustughfani are given. Then specific terms are defined: 'al-Hasan' in fiqh works refers to Hasan ibn Ziyad (the student of Abu Hanifa), not Hasan al-Basri (tafsir). 'al-Imam' or 'al-Imam al-A'zam' = Abu Hanifa. 'al-Sahibayn' = Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. 'al-Shaykhayn' = Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. 'al-Tarafayn' = Muhammad and Abu Hanifa. 'al-Imam al-Thani' = Abu Yusuf. 'al-Imam al-Rabbani' = Muhammad. 'al-A'immah al-Thalathah' = Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, Abu Yusuf. 'al-A'immah al-Arba'ah' = the four founders. 'Shams al-A'imma' = al-Sarakhsi. These definitions are crucial for reading Hanafi texts, as they specify exactly which scholars are being referenced by these common titles.

Key Terms
الخلف (al-khalaf): Later Hanafi scholars from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani to Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani.
السلف (al-salaf): Early Hanafi scholars from Abu Hanifa to Muhammad al-Shaybani.
المتأخرون (al-muta'akhkhirun): Latecomer Hanafi scholars from al-Halwani to Hafiz al-Din al-Bukhari; but sometimes applied to those before al-Halwani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which scholar is considered the start of the al-Muta'akhkhirun period according to the standard Hanafi classification?
A) Muhammad al-Shaybani
B) Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani
C) Hafiz al-Din al-Bukhari
D) Abu Hanifa

Key Takeaway
Scholarly periods in Hanafi works have defined boundaries but can be applied flexibly; always read contextually.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Key Individual Titles: Al-Imam, Al-Hasan, Al-Imam al-Thani, Al-Imam al-Rabbani, Shams al-A'imma
Defines individual titles for specific Hanafi scholars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which specific Hanafi scholar is intended by each of these five titles when used absolutely in Hanafi fiqh books.

Core Idea
Titles like al-Imam, al-Hasan, and Shams al-A'imma are not generic; each points to a specific Hanafi authority.

When you see 'al-Imam' in a Hanafi fiqh text, it almost always means Abu Hanifa. But 'al-Hasan' in fiqh is not Hasan al-Basri but Hasan ibn Ziyad. This lesson clarifies five such individual titles that are crucial for correctly attributing opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify 'al-Imam' and 'al-Imam al-A'zam'
In Hanafi books, these terms refer exclusively to Abu Hanifa, the founder of the madhhab.
Also called Sahib al-Madhhab.
2
Identify 'al-Hasan'
When used absolutely in fiqh works, it is Hasan ibn Ziyad, a direct student of Abu Hanifa. In tafsir, it is Hasan al-Basri.
Ghayat al-Bayan explicitly states this distinction.
3
Identify 'al-Imam al-Thani'
This is Abu Yusuf, the second greatest imam after Abu Hanifa.
He was the chief qadi of Baghdad.
4
Identify 'al-Imam al-Rabbani'
This is Muhammad al-Shaybani, the master compiler of the madhhab.
The title 'Rabbani' indicates divine knowledge.
5
Identify 'Shams al-A'imma'
When used absolutely, it is Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, the prolific Hanafi jurist of the 5th century.
Author of al-Mabsut and other works.

Commentary
This passage is a collection of definitions (fawa'id) clarifying technical terms used in Hanafi fiqh literature. It begins by classifying Hanafi scholars into three periods: al-Salaf (from Abu Hanifa to Muhammad al-Shaybani), al-Khalaf (from Muhammad al-Shaybani to Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani), and al-Muta'akhkhirun (from al-Halwani to Hafiz al-Din al-Bukhari). These definitions are crucial for reading Hanafi texts, as they specify exactly which scholars are being referenced by these common titles.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-Hasan): When mentioned absolutely in Hanafi fiqh books, refers to Hasan ibn Ziyad (student of Abu Hanifa). In tafsir, refers to Hasan al-Basri.
الإمام (al-Imam): In Hanafi books, means Abu Hanifa, the founder of the madhhab.
الإمام الثاني (al-Imam al-Thani): Abu Yusuf.
الإمام الرباني (al-Imam al-Rabbani): Muhammad al-Shaybani.
شمس الأئمة (Shams al-A'immah): When used absolutely in Hanafi books, refers to Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Paired and Group Titles: Al-Sahibayn, Al-Shaykhayn, Al-Tarafayn, A'immatuna al-Thalathah, Al-A'immah al-Arba'ah
Defines paired and group titles for Hanafi scholars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific scholars included in each of these five paired or group titles used in Hanafi literature.

Core Idea
Hanafi texts use compact paired/group titles; each pair or set has a precise membership that must be known to understand which opinions are being contrasted.

Terms like 'al-Sahibayn' and 'al-Shaykhayn' appear frequently in Hanafi fiqh discussions. They are shorthand for specific pairs of scholars. This lesson distinguishes these titles so you can instantly know which imams are being referenced when you encounter them.

Guided Path
1
Identify 'al-Sahibayn'
The two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. They are the primary students of Abu Hanifa.
Often mentioned together when their view differs from Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify 'al-Shaykhayn'
The two shaykhs: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. This pair includes the founder and his closest disciple.
Distinct from al-Sahibayn; note the inclusion of Abu Hanifa.
3
Identify 'al-Tarafayn'
The two extremes: Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Hanifa. This title usually indicates a contrast between the student (Muhammad) and the teacher (Abu Hanifa).
Used when their opinions are at odds.
4
Identify 'A'immatuna al-Thalathah'
Our three imams: Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf. This is the core trio of the Hanafi school.
All three are considered the foundational authorities.
5
Identify 'al-A'immah al-Arba'ah'
The four imams: Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal. This refers to the founders of the four Sunni schools.
Used when comparing madhhabs.

(٦) لعله : إبراهيم بن إسماعيل بن أحمد بن إسحاق بن شيت ، أبو إسحاق، ركن الإسلام، الزاهد المعروف بالصفار (ت ٥٣٤هـ) . ينظر : «الفوائد البهية ( ص ٢٤).
(۷) من العناية شرح الهداية»(۲ : ۲۸۷).
(۸) «كتائب أعلام الأخيار»(ق۱۲۸/أ).

ΛΕ
عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
وكذا الرُّسْتُغْفَني : بضم الراء المهملة، وضم التاء المثناة الفوقية، بينهما سين مهملة ساكنة، وسكون الغين المعجمة وفتح الفاء نسبة إلى رُستغفن قرية بسمرقند ، واسمه علي بن سعيد ، متقدم على الحَلْوَانِي، فإنّ الرُّسْتُغْفَنِي من تلامذة أبي منصورٍ الماتريدي ، المتوفي سنة ثلاث وثلاثين وثلاثمئة. والله أعلم.
(1)~
فائدة
الحَسَن ؛ إذا ذكر مطلقاً في كتب أصحابنا فالمراد به ابن زياد، تلميذ أبي حنيفة ، وإذا ذكر مطلقاً في كتب التفسير فالمراد به الحسن البصري. كذا في «غاية البيان»(۲)
في «باب النفقات» نقلا عن شيخه برهان الدين الخريفعني.
فائدة
المراد : بالإمام، والإمام الأعظم ؛ في كتب أصحابنا : هو صاحب المذهب أبو حنيفة، وهو المراد بقولهم : صاحب المذهب.
والمراد بالصاحبين : أبو يوسف ومحمد. وبالشيخين : أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف.
وبالطرفين : محمد وأبو حنيفة.

المشهورة. فائدة
• شمس الأئمة ؛ عند الإطلاق في كتب أصحابنا يراد به شمس الأئمة السَّرَخْسِي،
Fifth Study: Benefits for one who studies fiqh books. Ahmad al-Nakri in Jami' al-Ulum quoting from the author of al-Khiyalat al-Latifa: 'Indeed, al-Khalaf (the later scholars) among the fuqaha is from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan to Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, and al-Salaf (the early scholars) from Abu Hanifa to Muhammad, and al-Muta'akhkhirun (the latecomers) from al-Halwani to Hafiz al-Din al-Bukhari.' End. (2) And al-Dhahabi mentioned in the opening of his book Mizan al-I'tidal fi Naqd Asma' al-Rijal: 'Indeed, the dividing line between the early and later scholars is the year 300 [AH].' (4) But what Abd al-Nabi mentioned contradicts [this], that they often apply al-Muta'akhkhirun to those before al-Halwani; for he said in al-Hidayah in Kitab al-Sawm in the discussion of qada' of fasting for the insane: 'This is the chosen position of some of the Muta'akhkhirun.' End (5). He said in al-Inayah: 'Among them are Abu Abd Allah al-Jurjani, al-Imam al-Rustughfani, and al-Zahid al-Saffar.' (1) End, even though al-Jurjani preceded al-Halwani, for al-Halwani is from the men of the fifth century, died in the year 452 or 449 or 448 after four hundred [AH] as will come. (v) And Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Yahya al-Jurjani died in the year 398 or 397 [AH]. As al-Kafawi and others mentioned. (1) He is Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Nasr al-Bukhari, Abu al-Fadl, Hafiz al-Din al-Kabir... (2) See al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya p. 412 and al-Madkhal p. 273. (3) He is Shams al-Din Abu Abd Allah Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Dhahabi... (4) End from al-Mizan 1:115. (5) From al-Hidayah 1:129. (6) Possibly: Ibrahim ibn Isma'il... (7) From al-Inayah Sharh al-Hidayah 2:287. (8) Kata'ib A'lam al-Akhyar f.128a. [Page 84] ... And likewise al-Rustughfani: with damm on the ra, damm on the ta, between them a sukun on the sin, sukun on the ghayn, and fatha on the fa, relative to Rustughfan a village in Samarkand, his name is Ali ibn Sa'id, preceded al-Halwani, for al-Rustughfani is from the students of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who died in the year 333 [AH]. And Allah knows best. (1) Benefit: Al-Hasan; when mentioned absolutely in our companions' books, what is meant is Ibn Ziyad, the student of Abu Hanifa, and when mentioned absolutely in tafsir books, what is meant is al-Hasan al-Basri. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan (2) in the chapter on nafaqat, quoting from his teacher Burhan al-Din al-Khwarizmi. Benefit: What is meant by al-Imam and al-Imam al-A'zam in our companions' books: he is the founder of the madhhab, Abu Hanifa, and he is meant by their saying: Sahib al-Madhhab. And what is meant by al-Sahibayn: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. And by al-Shaykhayn: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And by al-Tarafayn: Muhammad and Abu Hanifa. (3) And by al-Imam al-Thani: Abu Yusuf. And by al-Imam al-Rabbani: Muhammad. And by their saying: 'when our three imams' it is: Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf. And by al-A'immah al-Arba'ah: Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad; the founders of the well-known schools. Benefit: Shams al-A'imma; when used absolutely in our companions' books, what is meant is Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a collection of definitions (fawa'id) clarifying technical terms used in Hanafi fiqh literature. It begins by classifying Hanafi scholars into three periods: al-Salaf (from Abu Hanifa to Muhammad al-Shaybani), al-Khalaf (from Muhammad al-Shaybani to Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani), and al-Muta'akhkhirun (from al-Halwani to Hafiz al-Din al-Bukhari). Al-Dhahabi is cited as setting the year 300 AH as the boundary between early and later scholars, but a contradiction is noted: in al-Hidayah, 'some Muta'akhkhirun' includes scholars before al-Halwani like al-Jurjani and al-Rustughfani, showing that usage can vary. Biographical details for al-Jurjani and al-Rustughfani are given. Then specific terms are defined: 'al-Hasan' in fiqh works refers to Hasan ibn Ziyad (the student of Abu Hanifa), not Hasan al-Basri (tafsir). 'al-Imam' or 'al-Imam al-A'zam' = Abu Hanifa. 'al-Sahibayn' = Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. 'al-Shaykhayn' = Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. 'al-Tarafayn' = Muhammad and Abu Hanifa. 'al-Imam al-Thani' = Abu Yusuf. 'al-Imam al-Rabbani' = Muhammad. 'al-A'immah al-Thalathah' = Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, Abu Yusuf. 'al-A'immah al-Arba'ah' = the four founders. 'Shams al-A'imma' = al-Sarakhsi. These definitions are crucial for reading Hanafi texts, as they specify exactly which scholars are being referenced by these common titles.

Key Terms
الصاحبين (al-Sahibayn): The two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
الشيخين (al-Shaykhayn): The two shaykhs: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
الطرفين (al-Tarafayn): The two extremes: Muhammad and Abu Hanifa (often when their positions differ).
أئمتنا الثلاثة (A'immaturna al-Thalathah): Our three imams: Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf.
الأئمة الأربعة (al-A'immah al-Arba'ah): The four imams: Abu Hanifa, Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Who are the two scholars referred to as 'al-Sahibayn'?
A) Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
B) Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani
C) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Abu Yusuf and Hasan ibn Ziyad

Key Takeaway
Paired/group titles in Hanafi works have precise memberships: al-Sahibayn = Abu Yusuf + Muhammad; al-Shaykhayn = Abu Hanifa + Abu Yusuf; al-Tarafayn = Muhammad + Abu Hanifa; A'immatuna al-Thalathah = the three founders; al-A'immah al-Arba'ah = the four Sunni imams.

Who Are 'Our Jurists' in Hanafi Texts?
Biographical clarification of a key term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four specific jurists meant by 'فقهائنا' in the Hanafi school and explain the rationale for including Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
In Hanafi texts, 'our jurists' means the three imams plus Abdullah ibn Mas'ud — a precise referent.

When you read 'فقهائنا' in a Hanafi fiqh book, do you know which scholars are being invoked? This lesson clarifies that the term is not generic but refers to a specific set of founding authorities. Understanding this ensures you correctly trace rulings to their sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its context
When you encounter 'فقهائنا' in Hanafi works, recognize it as a school-specific designation, not a general term for all Hanafi scholars.
This term appears frequently in matn and sharh texts.
2
List the four jurists
As per the passage, 'فقهائنا' refers to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.
The first three are the main imams; Ibn Mas'ud is a Companion.
3
Explain the inclusion of a Companion
Abdullah ibn Mas'ud is included because his legal opinions are foundational to the Hanafi school, especially in areas where he transmitted from the Prophet ﷺ.
This shows the school's reliance on Sahaba fiqh.
4
Note the philological detail
The passage also mentions the irregular plural 'عبادلة' (from 'عبدل'), showing the authors' precision in terminology.
This demonstrates the scholarly care in defining terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عند فقهائنا، فيراد بهم الثلاثة الأوّل مع عبد الله بن مسعود على ما فصلناه في ذيل «مقدمة الهداية» المسمى بـ«مذيلة الدراية». وهو جمع عبدل، بفتح العين، مخفف عبد الله، على خلاف القياس، كما بسطه الوالد العلام أدخله الله في دار السلام في حاشية نور الأنوار المسماة بـ«قمر الأقمار».
As for according to our jurists, what is meant by them are the first three [Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad] along with Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, as we detailed in the appendix of 'Muqaddimat al-Hidayah' called 'Mudhayyalat al-Dirayah'. And it is the plural of 'Abdul,' with fatha on the 'ayn, abbreviated from 'Abdullah,' irregularly, as explained by my father, the scholar, may Allah admit him into the abode of peace, in his gloss on 'Nur al-Anwar' called 'Qamar al-Aqmar'.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a biographical clarification of the term 'فقهائنا' (our jurists) as used in the Hanafi school. The author states that when the Hanafi texts say 'our jurists', they refer specifically to the three primary imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani—along with Abdullah ibn Mas'ud. This inclusion of Ibn Mas'ud is notable because he is a Companion whose fiqh is considered foundational. The author then notes that the word 'عبادلة' (the plural of 'عبدل') is an irregular diminutive form of 'عبد الله'. He references his father's work 'Qamar al-Aqmar' for further detail. The purpose is to ensure the reader understands exactly which authorities are being invoked.

Key Terms
فقهائنا (fuqaha'una): A term used in Hanafi texts to refer to the founding jurists of the school, specifically Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The term 'فقهائنا' in Hanafi works precisely denotes the three primary imams and Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.

Biography of Mahmud Pasha al-Wazir and His Hashiah
Opening of biographies of Wiqayah commentators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the key biographical details of Mahmud Pasha al-Wazir and explain the significance of his hashiah in the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Mahmud Pasha's hashiah on Sadr al-Shari'ah's commentary is a valuable link in the Hanafi scholarly chain.

This lesson introduces Mahmud Pasha al-Wazir, a 9th-century Hanafi scholar who compiled a biographical dictionary of Wiqayah commentators and authored a hashiah on Sadr al-Shari'ah's commentary. His work represents the ongoing tradition of super-commentary that preserved and refined Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the person and his major work
Find the name of the scholar and the title of the hashiah he wrote. Note the base text being commented on.
The base text is 'Sharh al-Wiqayah' by Sadr al-Shari'ah.
2
Locate his death date and place
Read the text to find where and when Mahmud Pasha died. Relate this to the broader historical context of the Ottoman Empire.
Constantinople (Istanbul) was a major center of Hanafi learning.
3
Understand the genre: hashiah
Define the term 'hashiah' as a gloss or super-commentary. Reflect on its role in Hanafi scholarship – correcting, expanding, or clarifying earlier commentaries.
Hashiahs often interact with earlier layers of commentary.
4
Trace the sources cited
Note the two works mentioned (al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya and Madinat al-Ulum) and the author's own work al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya. Understand how scholars cross-reference biographies.
These sources are biographical dictionaries of Hanafi scholars.
5
Recognize the intellectual context
Understand that this passage is part of a larger work collecting biographies of Wiqayah commentators. Appreciate the importance of knowing the human chain of transmission in Hanafi fiqh.
Biographical studies (tarajim) are a key part of Hanafi scholarship.

الدراسة السابعة: تراجم شراح الوقاية الوزراء، ألّفه لمحمود باشا الوزير. وله «حاشية على شرح الوقاية لصدر الشريعة وغير ذلك، وكانت وفاته بقسطنطينية، سنة خمس وسبعين وثمانمئة. كذا في الشقائق النعمانية». و«مدينة العلوم»، وقد بسطت الكلام في ترجمته مع التنبيه على زلات الكفــوي وغيره في «الفوائد البهية».
The seventh study: Biographies of the commentator-ministers of Al-Wiqayah, composed by Mahmud Pasha al-Wazir. Commentary
This passage opens the seventh study (al-dirasah al-sabi'ah) of the work, dedicated to the biographies of scholar-ministers (wuzara') who wrote commentaries on the famous Hanafi text Al-Wiqayah. The author of this section is Mahmud Pasha al-Wazir, who himself composed a hashiah (gloss) on Sadr al-Shari'ah's commentary of Al-Wiqayah. He died in Constantinople in 875 AH. The author references two sources: al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya and Madinat al-Ulum, and notes that he provided more detail in his own work al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya, where he also pointed out errors made by al-Kafawi and others.

Biographies of Hanafi Commentators on Al-Wiqayah
Biographical listing of Hanafi commentators on Al-Wiqayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify key Hanafi scholars who wrote commentaries on Al-Wiqayah and explain the importance of their biographical lineage in the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Knowing the chain of scholars who commented on Al-Wiqayah ensures the reliability of transmitted rulings.

This lesson introduces you to the scholars who dedicated themselves to explaining Al-Wiqayah, a foundational Hanafi fiqh text. Their commentaries are not just explanations; they represent a living chain of knowledge that connects us to the early authorities. Understanding their biographies helps assess the weight of their interpretations in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify each scholar
Read the passage and list each commentator's full name, title, and death date. Note any descriptive epithets like 'al-Allamah' or 'Shaykh al-Islam'.
Focus on the four main figures: al-Karmasani, al-Qujawi, al-Tamartashi, and al-Harawi.
2
Map the chain of transmission
For each scholar, identify their teachers as mentioned (e.g., al-Tamartashi studied under Zayn ibn Nujaym). Trace how knowledge was passed down.
The chain is crucial for assessing authority; see al-Tamartashi's teachers from Egypt.
3
Catalog major works
List the commentaries (shuruh) and other writings each scholar produced, especially those on Al-Wiqayah.
For example, al-Qujawi wrote 'Sharh al-Wiqayah' and al-Tamartashi wrote 'Minh al-Ghaffar'.
4
Recognize the nisba and geography
Note the nisba (name suffix indicating origin) for each scholar, e.g., al-Tamartashi from Tumurtash in Khwarazm. Understand how geography influenced their scholarship.
Al-Tamartashi's nisba is explained in detail.
5
Understand the significance
Reflect on why the author included these biographies. They establish the reliability of each commentary and the scholarly consensus behind them.
This is a memorization anchor: knowing the scholars helps you later when citing their works.

الدراسة السابعة: تراجم شراح الوقاية ومتن في الكلام وشرحه ومتن في المعاني والبيان وشرحه، ومتن في الفرائض وشرحه، وحاشية على شرح المفتاح» وعلى «الهداية»، وعلى «تهافت الفلاسفة» لخواجه زاده وغير ذلك، وكانت وفاته بقسطنطينية وهو مفت بها سنة أربعين وتسعمئة. كذا في أعلام الأخيار، وغيره، وقد ذكرنا ترجمته مبسوطة في «الفوائد البهية في تراجم الحنفية»). (1) . ومنهم: يوسف بن حسين الكرماسني، المتوفى في حدود سنة تسعمئة، سمّى شرحه بـ«الحماية على شرح الوقاية ) ، وسيأتي ذكره عند ذكر محشي «شرح الوقاية». Commentary
This passage is a biographical section listing several Hanafi scholars who wrote commentaries on the Hanafi fiqh manual 'Al-Wiqayah' by Sadr al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi. The section is titled 'The Seventh Study: Biographies of Commentators of Al-Wiqayah'. It first mentions a scholar whose biography was previously detailed in 'Al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyyah', then lists Yusuf ibn Husayn al-Karmasani (d. 900 AH), author of 'Al-Himayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah'. Next is Muhammad ibn Muslih al-Din al-Qujawi (d. 950 AH), known as Sheikh Zadeh al-Rumi, who authored several commentaries and glosses. Then the text provides a detailed biography of Muhammad ibn Abdullah al-Tamartashi (d. 1004 AH), the author of 'Tanwir al-Absar' and its commentary 'Minh al-Ghaffar'. It lists his teachers, works, and death date. Finally, it mentions Fasih al-Din al-Harawi, whose commentary on Al-Wiqayah the author studied, and notes that al-Harawi's maternal grandfather was the famous Shaykh al-Taslim, who wrote a treatise on iman. The passage serves to establish the scholarly lineage and authority of these commentators.

Key Terms
الوقاية (Al-Wiqayah): A foundational Hanafi fiqh text by Sadr al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi upon which many commentaries were written.
تراجم (Tarajim): Biographies or biographical entries documenting the lives and works of scholars.
شرح الوقاية (Sharh al-Wiqayah): A commentary on the fiqh text Al-Wiqayah. Many such commentaries exist; this passage lists several authors.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yusuf bin Husayn al-Karmasani

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school preserves its teachings through a chain of scholars; knowing their biographies is essential for determining the reliability of transmitted rulings.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
The Importance of Correct Attribution: Authorship of Dhakhirat al-Uqba and the Commentators of Sharh al-Wiqaya
Biographical correction on Dhakhirat al-Uqba and listing of Sharh al-Wiqaya glossators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why identifying the correct author of a Hanafi fiqh work is critical for the reliability (riwaya) of its rulings, and list the key Hanafi scholars who authored glosses on Sharh al-Wiqaya.

Core Idea
A work's authority depends on its author's chain — misattribution can weaken a transmitted ruling.

This lesson dives into a biographical correction from the front matter of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. The author argues that the fiqh work Dhakhirat al-Uqba is not by the well-known scholar Hasan Jalabi al-Fanari but by his brother Akhi Jalabi Yusuf. We will examine the evidence and then explore a list of later Hanafi scholars who wrote glosses on Sharh al-Wiqaya, a key text in the school. Understanding these attributions is essential for assessing the authority of rulings found in these works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the authorship debate
The passage opens by stating that Dhakhirat al-Uqba is not by Hasan Jalabi al-Fanari, contrary to some claims. This is the core issue: who wrote this fiqh text?
2
Examine the biographical evidence
The author cites three major biographical works: Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya, al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya, and Kashf al-Zunun. These sources attribute to Hasan Jalabi works like Hawashi al-Mutawwal, but attribute Dhakhirat al-Uqba to his brother Akhi Jalabi Yusuf.
Biographical dictionaries are key tools for verifying authorship.
3
Analyze the stylistic argument
The author argues that Hasan Jalabi's works are marked by profound investigations and subtle clarifications, while Dhakhirat al-Uqba lacks such depth and shows a weaker scholarly faculty. This internal evidence supports the external biographical data.
Stylistic comparison is a common method in Islamic scholarship.
4
List the commentators on Sharh al-Wiqaya
The passage then lists several Hanafi scholars who wrote glosses (hawashi) on Sharh al-Wiqaya: Khatibzadeh al-Rumi, al-Niksari, al-Karmasani, al-'Ajami, Husamzadeh, and al-Fanari. Note which of them completed their works.
These glosses are secondary sources that often contain tarjih and takhrij.
5
Understand the relevance for fiqh
Correctly attributing a work affects the weight given to its rulings. If a ruling is cited from Dhakhirat al-Uqba, knowing it is by Akhi Jalabi (not Hasan Jalabi) helps assess it within the Hanafi hierarchy of authorities.
Authority (riwaya) is a function of the author's chain and reliability.

الدراسة الثامنة تراجم محشي شرح الوقاية

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya. Hasan Jalabi al-Fanari, and Mawlana 'Arab, and others. End. This is a text establishing that the author of 'al-Dhakhira' is not Hasan Jalabi. Also, whoever studies 'Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya' by al-Kafawi, 'al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya', 'Kashf al-Zunun', etc., knows with certainty that the author of 'al-Dhakhira' is not Hasan Jalabi, for they mention Hasan Jalabi and count among his works 'Hawashi al-Mutawwal' and others, and they mention Akhi Jalabi Yusuf and attribute to him 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba' and a treatise collecting issues related to disbelief, titled 'Hidayat al-Muhtadin'. Also, anyone with strong perception and discernment knows from studying 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba' and from studying Hasan Jalabi's works that it belongs to another; for all of Hasan Jalabi's works contain profound investigations and subtle clarifications, testifying to the erudition of their author and the brilliance of his nature, unlike 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba', which contains nothing to slake the thirst or cure the ailing, let alone those investigations and clarifications; it contains things testifying that its author lacks a firmly rooted faculty and complete ability. Among them: al-Mawla Muhyi al-Din Muhammad, known as Khatibzadeh al-Rumi; he studied under his father Taj al-Din, 'Ali al-Tusi, Khidr Beg, and other eminent scholars of his country; became a teacher at one of the eight madrasas in Constantinople; then Sultan Muhammad Khan made him his personal tutor; he died in the year 901 AH. He authored: 'Hawashi 'ala Hashiyat al-Sayyid al-muta'alliqa bi-Sharh al-Tajrid', 'Hawashi 'ala Hashiyat al-Kashshaf' by al-Sayyid, 'Hawashi 'ala Hashiyat Sharh al-Mukhtasar' by al-Sayyid, 'Risala fi Bahth al-Ru'ya wa al-Kalam', 'Hawashi 'ala Sharh al-Mawaqif', 'Hawashi 'ala al-Muqaddimat al-Arba' min al-Tawdih', etc. He authored a 'Hashiya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya' but did not complete it. Thus in 'al-A'lam' and 'al-Shaqa'iq'. Among them: Muhyi al-Din Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Husayn al-Niksari al-Rumi; he was knowledgeable in the religious sciences and rational arts; studied under Husam al-Din al-Tawqati and Yusuf... Our shaykh... It seems that this phrase is from the marginalia of Akhi Jalabi on 'al-Dhakhira', but I have not come across it; for these marginalia are not written on my copies of 'al-Dhakhira', both of which are Indian lithographic prints. And Allah knows best.

The Eighth Study: Biographies of the Commentators of Sharh al-Wiqaya

Among them: Yusuf ibn Husayn al-Karmasani; he studied under Khwajazadeh and other scholars of his time, excelled in the sciences, became a teacher in Constantinople, then a judge in the city of Edirne; he authored 'Hawashi Sharh al-Wiqaya', 'Hawashi al-Mutawwal', and others; he died around the year 900 AH. Thus in 'al-Shaqa'iq'. Among them: Muhyi al-Din Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-'Ajami; he was a virtuous scholar, a teacher at one of the eight madrasas, then a judge in Edirne and died there; he authored 'Risala 'ala Bab al-Shahid min Sharh al-Wiqaya' and 'Hawashi 'ala Sharh al-Sirajiyya' by al-Sayyid. Thus in 'al-A'lam'. Disciples: Among them: Muslih al-Din Mustafa ibn Husam al-Din, known as Husamzadeh, from 'Ala' al-Din al-Jamali, student of Mulla Khusraw, author of 'al-Durar'; he was skilled in literary sciences, knowledgeable in religious sciences; among his works: 'Hashiyat Sharh al-Wiqaya' and 'Musannaf fi al-Insha''. Thus in 'al-A'lam'. Among them: Muhyi al-Din Muhammad Shah ibn 'Ali ibn Yusuf Bali ibn Shams al-Din Muhammad ibn Hamza al-Fanari; he studied under his father and under Khatibzadeh, became a teacher in Bursa.

Commentary
This passage is a biographical section from the introduction of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya', identifying the correct author of the work 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba' (often attributed to Hasan Jalabi) and listing several Hanafi scholars who composed glosses (hawashi) on Sharh al-Wiqaya. The author begins by clarifying that 'al-Dhakhira' (meaning Dhakhirat al-Uqba) is not by Hasan Jalabi al-Fanari, contrary to some claims. He provides evidence from biographical works such as Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya, al-Shaqa'iq al-Nu'maniyya, and Kashf al-Zunun, which attribute to Hasan Jalabi only certain works like Hawashi al-Mutawwal, and attribute Dhakhirat al-Uqba to his brother Akhi Jalabi Yusuf. The author further argues from stylistic and substantive differences between Hasan Jalabi's profound works and the weaker content of Dhakhirat al-Uqba. Then he proceeds to list several Hanafi scholars who wrote hashiyas on Sharh al-Wiqaya: 1) al-Mawla Muhyi al-Din Muhammad Khatibzadeh al-Rumi (d. 901 AH), who studied with his father and other scholars, taught at a madrasa in Constantinople, and was personal tutor to Sultan Muhammad Khan; he authored many glosses and began a Hashiya on Sharh al-Wiqaya but did not complete it. 2) Muhyi al-Din Muhammad al-Niksari al-Rumi, who studied under Husam al-Din al-Tawqati. The text mentions a marginal note by 'our shaykh' suggesting the phrase is from Akhi Jalabi's marginalia on al-Dhakhira, but the author notes he has not seen it on his Indian lithographic copies. 3) Yusuf ibn Husayn al-Karmasani (d. ~900 AH), who studied under Khwajazadeh, taught in Constantinople, became a judge in Edirne, and authored Hawashi Sharh al-Wiqaya. 4) Muhyi al-Din Ahmad al-'Ajami, who also taught and judged in Edirne, authored a treatise on the chapter of the martyr from Sharh al-Wiqaya and glosses on Sharh al-Sirajiyya. 5) Muslih al-Din Mustafa Husamzadeh, a student of 'Ala' al-Din al-Jamali and Mulla Khusraw, authored a Hashiya on Sharh al-Wiqaya. 6) Muhyi al-Din Muhammad Shah al-Fanari, who studied under his father and Khatibzadeh, became a teacher in Bursa. This section serves as a scholarly introduction establishing the authority and provenance of the works used in the commentary.

Key Terms
الذخيرة (al-Dhakhira): Refers specifically to 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba', a Hanafi fiqh work whose authorship is debated; the text argues it is not by Hasan Jalabi but by his brother Akhi Jalabi Yusuf.
حواشي شرح الوقاية (Hawashi Sharh al-Wiqaya): Glosses or supercommentaries on Sharh al-Wiqaya (a commentary on al-Wiqaya, a Hanafi fiqh manual). These were composed by various later Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who is the correct author of Dhakhirat al-Uqba?
A) Hasan Jalabi al-Fanari
B) Akhi Jalabi Yusuf
C) Khatibzadeh al-Rumi
D) Yusuf al-Karmasani

Key Takeaway
Correct attribution of a fiqh work is essential for assessing the authority of its rulings; the case of Dhakhirat al-Uqba illustrates how biographical and stylistic evidence can resolve authorship disputes in the Hanafi tradition.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
The Commentator of Sharh al-Wiqayah: Scholarly Lineage and Works
Biographical sketch with teacher list and bibliography.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key teachers and the ijazah chain of the commentator of Sharh al-Wiqayah, and list the major categories of his authored works.

Core Idea
A scholar's ijazah and teacher connections authenticate his authority in transmitting Hanafi knowledge.

This lesson explores the biographical introduction to 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. The passage details the scholarly network of the commentator, his teachers, his ijazah, and his extensive writings. Understanding this background helps you appreciate the authority behind a Hanafi commentary and the breadth of a scholar's contribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the teachers mentioned
Read the list of teachers: Shaykh 'Ali al-Hariri al-Madani, Mawlana 'Abd al-Ghani al-Mujaddidi, Mawlana 'Abd al-Rashid al-Mujaddidi, and Mawlana Husayn Ahmad al-Malihabadi. Note their titles like 'Shaykh al-Dala'il' and 'Muhaddith' to gauge their scholarly status.
This shows the commentator's direct link to the Naqshbandi-Mujaddidi tradition.
2
Trace the ijazah chain
The commentator had an earlier ijazah from Mawlana Husayn Ahmad al-Malihabadi, who was a student of Shaykh 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Dihlawi. This chain connects to the renowned Dihlawi school.
Ijazah ensures authenticity in transmission, a key feature of Hanafi learning.
3
Recognize the commentator's career and death
He returned to Hyderabad, was appointed chief judge of the high court, and died in 1285 AH. This shows the practical application of his knowledge.
His judicial role indicates his expertise in fiqh.
4
Survey the list of works
The passage lists many works: on fiqh (e.g., 'Ghayat al-Kalam fi Bayan al-Halal wa al-Haram'), hadith, kalam, logic, medicine, and more. Note that some works are incomplete.
This demonstrates the scholarly breadth expected of a Hanafi commentator.

The Eighth Study: Biographies of the Commentator of Sharh al-Wiqayah
Shaykh al-Dala'il: Shaykh 'Ali al-Hariri al-Madani, the deceased. And Mawlana 'Abd al-Ghani al-Mujaddidi al-Dihlawi, died in the year ninety-six [1296?]. And Mawlana 'Abd al-Rashid al-Mujaddidi (3), and others.
He had a prior ijazah from Mawlana Husayn Ahmad al-Muhaddith al-Malihabadi, student of Shaykh Mawlana 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Dihlawi.
Then he returned to Hyderabad in the year eighty [1280?] in Rabi' al-Akhir, so the praised vizier entrusted to him the administration of the high judiciary of the diwan, so he turned to settling disputes with good order until he died there on Monday the 29th of Sha'ban of the year eighty-five [1285?]. He has many authored works:
... [list of works and biographical notes]
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a biographical section (tarajim) from the introduction of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. It provides the scholarly lineage, teachers, and a detailed list of works of the commentator (muhashshi) on Sharh al-Wiqayah. The author first mentions his teachers: Shaykh 'Ali al-Hariri al-Madani (known as Shaykh al-Dala'il), Mawlana 'Abd al-Ghani al-Mujaddidi al-Dihlawi, and Mawlana 'Abd al-Rashid al-Mujaddidi. He then notes that he received an earlier ijazah from Mawlana Husayn Ahmad al-Malihabadi, who was a student of the famous scholar 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Dihlawi. The passage then describes his return to Hyderabad and appointment as chief judge, and his death. The bulk of the text is a list of his numerous writings, covering fiqh, hadith, kalam, logic, medicine, and other sciences. Many of these works are in the form of commentaries and glosses on standard texts. The author also mentions a separate biography he wrote about himself titled 'Hasrat al-'Alam' and promises a fuller biography in his work 'Khayr al-'Amal'. This section is important for understanding the scholarly context and breadth of the commentator's expertise.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijāzah): A license or certification from a scholar authorizing the recipient to transmit certain teachings or texts.
تراجم (tarājim): Biographies; a genre of Islamic literature collecting life accounts of scholars.
محشي (muḥashshī): A commentator or glossator who writes marginal notes (hashiyah) on a primary text.
العدالة العالية الديوانية (al-‘adālah al-‘āliyah al-dīwāniyah): The high judiciary of the diwan; likely the chief justice position in the Hyderabad state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Mawlana 'Abd al-Rashid al-Mujaddidi

Key Takeaway
The commentator's scholarly authority is validated by his teachers, ijazah chain, and prolific output across multiple disciplines.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
6 min
Defending Abu Hanifa's Worship: The Hanafi Response to the Bid'ah Accusation
Defending Abu Hanifa's virtues and refuting accusations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers Abu Hanifa's intense worship not to be bid'ah, using the precedent of earlier scholars.

Core Idea
Intense worship practiced by the Salaf cannot be bid'ah; if it were, the best generations would be innovators.

In this passage, the author defends Imam Abu Hanifa against accusations that his intense worship—such as praying one thousand rak'ahs in a night—is an innovation. The author argues that such worship has precedent among the Companions, Successors, and later Imams, and therefore cannot be considered blameworthy innovation. This lesson will help you understand the Hanafi methodology for evaluating acts of worship and responding to bid'ah claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation
Note the critic's claim: that Abu Hanifa's practices like staying up all night, completing the Quran in one night, and praying many rak'ahs are bid'ah, citing the hadith 'kullu bid'atin dalalah'.
The critic equates any new practice with misguidance.
2
Examine the Hanafi response
The author dismisses the accusation as coming from one lacking understanding. He wrote a treatise on the topic and then provides a list of eminent scholars who performed similar acts of worship.
The response is based on precedent (athar) and analogy (qiyas).
3
Analyze the list of precedents
The author names Companions like Uthman, Ibn Umar, and later Imams like al-Shafi'i. He argues that if such worship were bid'ah, all these figures would be innovators, which is absurd.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Understand the Hanafi definition of bid'ah
The Hanafi school distinguishes between innovation in matters of worship (bid'ah dalalah) and innovation in worldly matters (bid'ah 'adiyyah). Acts based on general principles of devotion are not blameworthy.
The author's argument implies that intense worship is covered by general Quranic commands to worship.

عثمان، وابن عمر، وشداد بن أوس ، وتميم الداري ، وعبد الله بن الزبير ه ، ومسروق، وعبد الرحمن بن الأسود، وعمرو بن ميمون، وسعيد بن جبير، وسعيد بن المسيب، وخالد بن معدان، وأبو اسحاق السبيعي، ووهب بن منبه، والإمام محمد الباقر، والإمام زين العابدين علي بن حسين، والإمام السجاد علي بن عبد الله. وأويس القرني، وقتادة، وثابت البناني، وصلة بن أشيم)، وعروة بن الزبير، وابن عساكر، والخطيب البغدادي ، وعبد الغني المقدسي وعمير بن هانئ، وعامر بن عبد الله، والأسود النخعي، ومالك بن دينار، ومنصور بن زاذان، وسليمان التيمي ، ومحمد بن واسع، والإمام الشافعي، وأبو بكر بن عياش.
ومسعر بن كدام، وعبد الله بن إدريس ، وأبو يوسف القاضي، ويحيى بن سعيد القطان، ووكيع بن الجراح، وبشر بن مفضل، ويزيد بن هارون، وعبد الرحمن بن مهدي، وهناد بن السري والأوزاعي، وسليمان بن طَرْخان، وأيوب السختياني، وصفوان بن سليم، وحسن بن صالح، وإسماعيل بن عياش ، وغيرهم" كما لا يخفى على من طالع تراجمهم في تذكرة الحفاظ» و«مرآة الجنان»، وكتاب «الأنساب»، و«حلية الأولياء»، و«سير النبلاء، فإن كان الإكثار في العبادة مطلقاً بدعة، لزم كون هؤلاء الأكابر من أهل البدعة ومن يلتزم ذلك فهو أضل الجاهلين وأكبر الفاسقين.
وأما تصانيف أبي حنيفة .
فذكروا منها : «الفقه الأكبر» و«كتاب الوصية»، و«كتاب العالم والمتعلم
and 'al-Intisar li Imam A'immat al-Amsar', and 'al-Bustan fi Manaqib al-Nu'man'), and other books and notebooks that the great hadith scholars and elders authored. That is relied upon. If someone says: These virtues that they mentioned are all without chain, and such an argument is not accepted. We say: No, rather they are with chain in 'Hilyat al-Awliya' by Abu Nu'aym al-Isfahani, and 'Tarikh al-Khatib al-Baghdadi', and other books of chains by masters of documentation, even though those who mention these beautiful descriptions and convey these lofty praises are the pillars of Islam to whom people turn, whose statements are relied upon, and whose transmissions are accepted in the field of biographies, reports, and rulings. This is sufficient to prove his merit. And do not think as some of the virtuous of our time thought in 'Ithaf al-Nubala' and his followers and imitators that such praises are from the exaggeration of the Hanafis. Indeed, they are not unique in transmitting them; rather, the hadith scholars, historians, and reliable authorities have affirmed them. If one objects that abundant worship, such as staying up the whole night, completing the entire Quran in one night, and performing one thousand rak'ahs and the like, is an innovation, and every innovation is misguidance. We say: This is the statement of one who has no understanding and no support, as I have established in my treatise 'Iqamat al-Hujjah anna al-Ikthar fi al-Ta'abbud laysa bi Bid'ah': that striving in worship is neither innovation nor misguidance, especially since the striving in worship transmitted from Abu Hanifa is proven similarly from many of the Companions, Successors, Mujtahid Imams, and Hadith scholars: among them: Uthman, Ibn Umar, Shaddad ibn Aws, Tamim al-Dari, Abdullah ibn al-Zubayr, Masruq, Abd al-Rahman ibn al-Aswad, Amr ibn Maymun, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, Khalid ibn Ma'dan, Abu Ishaq al-Sabi'i, Wahb ibn Munabbih, Imam Muhammad al-Baqir, Imam Zayn al-Abidin Ali ibn Husayn, Imam al-Sajjad Ali ibn Abdullah, Uways al-Qarani, Qatada, Thabit al-Bunani, Silah ibn Ashim, Urwa ibn al-Zubayr, Ibn Asakir, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, Abd al-Ghani al-Maqdisi, Umayr ibn Hani', Amir ibn Abdullah, al-Aswad al-Nakha'i, Malik ibn Dinar, Mansur ibn Zadhan, Sulayman al-Taymi, Muhammad ibn Wasi', Imam al-Shafi'i, Abu Bakr ibn Ayyash, Mis'ar ibn Kidam, Abdullah ibn Idris, Abu Yusuf al-Qadi, Yahya ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, Waki' ibn al-Jarrah, Bishr ibn al-Mufaddal, Yazid ibn Harun, Abd al-Rahman ibn Mahdi, Hannad ibn al-Sari, al-Awza'i, Sulayman ibn Tarkhan, Ayyub al-Sakhtiyani, Safwan ibn Sulaym, Hasan ibn Salih, Isma'il ibn Ayyash, and others, as is not hidden from one who examines their biographies in 'Tadhkirat al-Huffaz', 'Mir'at al-Jinan', 'Kitab al-Ansab', 'Hilyat al-Awliya', and 'Siyar al-Nubala'. If abundant worship absolutely were innovation, it would necessarily follow that these great figures are among the people of innovation, and whoever adheres to that is the most misguided of the ignorant and the greatest of the wicked. As for the works of Abu Hanifa, they mentioned among them: 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar', 'Kitab al-Wasiyyah', and 'Kitab al-'Alim wa al-Muta'allim'.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a vigorous defense of Imam Abu Hanifa's personal merits and religious practice. The author begins by affirming that the numerous virtues narrated about Abu Hanifa are authentic, as they appear in well-known hadith and history books like Hilyat al-Awliya and Tarikh Baghdad, which were written by respected scholars. The author then anticipates a criticism: that these virtues are excessive praise by Hanafis. He rejects this, stating that non-Hanafi authorities also transmit them. Next, the author addresses a second criticism: that Abu Hanifa's intense worship—such as praying a thousand rak'ahs in one night or completing the Quran in one recitation—is an innovation (bid'ah) and therefore misguidance. The author dismisses this accusation by claiming it comes from someone lacking true understanding. He devotes a treatise to the topic and presents an argument from precedent: many leading figures from the Companions, Successors, and later imams, including Uthman, Ibn Umar, and even Imam al-Shafi'i, engaged in similar strenuous worship. If such worship were bid'ah, all these eminent scholars would be innovators, which is absurd. Finally, the author briefly lists some of Abu Hanifa's written works—al-Fiqh al-Akbar, Kitab al-Wasiyyah, and Kitab al-'Alim wa al-Muta'allim—as evidence of his scholarly contributions.

Key Terms
بدعة (bid'ah): An innovation in religion that has no basis in the Quran or Sunnah; here, specifically applied to practices of intense worship.
سند (sanad): Chain of transmission; the sequence of narrators connecting a report to its source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is Abu Hanifa's extensive worship not considered bid'ah?
A) Because he only performed these acts on rare occasions.
B) Because many earlier scholars, including Companions and Imams, performed similar acts of worship.
C) Because bid'ah only applies to beliefs, not actions.
D) Because the Quran explicitly permits praying one thousand rak'ahs.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's intense worship is not bid'ah because it has clear precedent among the Salaf; the Hanafi school defends it as a legitimate expression of devotion.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Biographical Entry: Khubayb ibn 'Adi (Habib Allah)
Biographical heading and entry for Khubayb ibn 'Adi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Khubayb ibn 'Adi and state his significance in Hanafi fiqh as a reference in the chapter of coercion (ikrah).

Core Idea
Khubayb ibn 'Adi, a Badr veteran, was the first martyr crucified in Islam for spying — his story grounds Hanafi rulings on coercion.

This lesson introduces the biographical section of our text, which provides context for figures cited in legal discussions. The first entry is Habib Allah, better known as Khubayb ibn 'Adi. Though his story is not a legal ruling, understanding his life helps anchor the fiqh discussions on ikrah where his case is referenced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the person
The text gives the name as Habib Allah, but reveals his actual name is Khubayb ibn 'Adi, a Companion from the Ansar.
Habib Allah is a kunya meaning 'beloved of Allah'.
2
Determine his relation to the Prophet
He witnessed the Battle of Badr with the Prophet and was later sent as a spy by the Prophet.
3
Understand his martyrdom
He was captured by the Quraysh and crucified in Mecca, becoming the first person to be crucified in Islam, for the sake of Allah.
The text emphasizes his crucifixion was for the sake of Allah (fi dhat Allah).
4
Connect to fiqh context
Khubayb is mentioned in the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah) in the Hanafi fiqh text, indicating his case is used to illustrate rulings on coercion, such as the obligation to resist renouncing faith under duress.
5
Note the sources
The full story is recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari and Musnad Ahmad, showing the reliance on authentic hadith for legal references.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدراسة التاسعة: تراجم الأعيان في الشرح
حبيب الله : له ذكر في كتاب الإكراه»، هو خُبيب - مصغر ابن عدي بن مالك ابن عامر بن مجدعة الأنصاري الأوسي، شهد بدرا مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، وصلبه الكفّار بمكة حين أخذوه، وقد كان رسول الله له بعثه مع رجال آخرين عيناً، وهو أول مصلوب في الإسلام، وصلب في ذات الله، وقصة صلبه مبسوطة في «صحيح البخاري» و «مسند أحمد» وغيرهما من كتب الحديث.
The Ninth Study: Biographies of Notables in the Commentary. Habib Allah: He is mentioned in the Book of Coercion. He is Khubayb - diminutive - ibn 'Adi ibn Malik ibn 'Amir ibn Majda'ah al-Ansari al-Awsi. He witnessed Badr with the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. The disbelievers crucified him in Mecca when they captured him. The Messenger of Allah had sent him with other men as a spy. He was the first to be crucified in Islam, and he was crucified for the sake of Allah. His story of crucifixion is detailed in Sahih al-Bukhari, Musnad Ahmad, and other hadith books.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry from the section 'Tarajim al-A'yan fi al-Sharh' (Biographies of Notables in the Commentary). The author provides a brief biography of Habib Allah, whose actual name is Khubayb ibn 'Adi. He was a Companion of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) who participated in the Battle of Badr. He was captured by the Quraysh and crucified in Mecca, becoming the first martyr to be crucified in Islam. The author notes that the full account of his crucifixion can be found in major hadith collections such as Sahih al-Bukhari and Musnad Ahmad. This biography is included because Khubayb is referenced in the legal discussion of coercion (ikrah) in the fiqh text.

Key Terms
تراجم الأعيان (tarājim al-a‘yān): Biographical sketches of notable individuals referenced in the commentary.
حبيب الله (ḥabīb allāh): A Companion of the Prophet, also known as Khubayb ibn 'Adi, mentioned in the context of ikrah (coercion).
خبيب (khubayb): The actual name of Habib Allah, a diminutive form of 'Khabib'? meaning 'little friend'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Battle of Badr
B) Battle of Uhud
C) Battle of Khandaq
D) Battle of Hunayn

Key Takeaway
Khubayb ibn 'Adi is a Companion whose martyrdom by crucifixion is a key reference in Hanafi discussions on coercion (ikrah) — he refused to renounce faith under threat of death.
